*  which indicates top-tier pieces show up). If resort designers browse it regularly, they could snag unique items – but one has to monitor listings. For 30 suites, Place2Place could yield a handful of gems (especially if budget is available to buy those listed design icons). They don’t curate for you, but an interior designer could treat it as a shopping source.  
  They can allow purchasing of disparate items easily. The downside: one must ensure condition is as advertised; also they won't unify the look – that’s up to the designer’s eye.  
  With 20 weeks, one could collect items steadily. They also cover art/lighting categories, so might find a fancy chandelier here, a sculpture there.  
  Custom refurb: Place2Place itself doesn’t refurbish, so either the item is used in current state or the hotel separately re-finishes it.  
  Score: 2.5/5 – a good resource for unique finds, but requires significant involvement from the project’s design team to assemble a cohesive collection.

Summary Scenario 3: This luxury, eclectic scenario leans heavily on specialized reuse players with creative capacity. YLLW Factory and Input/Greenified stand out for delivering curated, high-quality, fully refurbished pieces with design cohesion

[alsbergstudio.se](https://alsbergstudio.se/repurposing-of-furniture/#:~:text=,interior%20with%20character%20and%20individuality)

[wihlborgs.se](https://www.wihlborgs.se/sv/om-oss/nyheter/hallbar-fastighetsutveckling/minskad-klimatpaverkan-med-aterbruk-av-kontorsmobler/#:~:text=dem%20klassiska%20designm%C3%B6bler%20i%20behov,l%C3%A4gre%20pris%20och%20l%C3%A4gre%20klimatp%C3%A5verkan)

. Kompanjonen can serve as the “scout” for truly unique or hard-to-find items, collaborating with those full-service players. RP and smaller upcyclers can contribute one-of-a-kind furniture art pieces. Likely a combination: e.g., Input provides the core furniture set for each suite (some reused classics, fully redone), and Kompanjonen sources a few singular antiques or artwork per suite; Yllw or RP handle custom refurbishment of those sourced antiques if needed. The resort ends up with a rich mix where nearly everything has a story, delivered within 20 weeks due to careful planning and division of labor.

### **Coordinating Multiple Operators**

In scenarios where no single operator covers all needs, a multi-supplier strategy can be used. For instance:

* Scenario 1 (Boutique 45-room): A strong combination would be YLLW Factory for bulk furniture (beds, chairs) and Kompanjonen or a local vintage shop for unique lobby decor, plus possibly a Norwegian logistics partner for quick delivery to Oslo. This minimizes risk (Yllw handles majority with warranty, Kompanjonen adds character pieces). Coordination complexity: moderate – need to align design choices between Yllw and the boutique items.  
* Scenario 2 (Chain 120-room): A likely trio is Input/Greenified as primary (for all major furniture, compliance), Återbruksfabriken for any building materials (if walls, doors being reused), and a linen rental service for towels. Additionally, Yllw or RP could supply some items if Input needs extra volume. Coordination complexity: high – would require clear split (e.g., Input does furniture, builder works with Återbruksfabriken on materials). A general contractor or consultant (maybe Kompanjonen acting as project manager) can help orchestrate the timeline – ensuring reused items are ready when needed on site (just-in-time to avoid storage issues).  
* Scenario 3 (Luxury resort): A curated alliance: YLLW Factory \+ Kompanjonen \+ local antique dealers/artists. Perhaps Yllw is lead contractor supplying refurbished furniture sets, Kompanjonen sources specific antiques and an art curator sources art; all items then sent to Yllw’s facility for any touch-ups and delivered together. The resort’s interior designer would coordinate aesthetic vision among these parties. Complexity: high, but acceptable given timeline and project scale (30 suites). Framework agreements can be set: e.g., Yllw and Kompanjonen both contract with the client under a shared sustainability goal.

Managing multiple vendors: It raises the need for: clear responsibilities, integrated scheduling (so one vendor’s delay doesn’t stall the entire fit-out), and possibly a prime contractor (could be the design firm or a consultant) to manage communications. However, leveraging multiple specialists can ensure each need is met by an expert, increasing the overall success of reuse in the project. In conclusion, the sourcing deep-dive shows that no single operator is perfect for all scenarios, but Sweden’s reuse ecosystem collectively can cover nearly every need if used in combination. Large full-service hubs handle volume and logistics, while niche players contribute specialized items or knowledge (compliance, unique sourcing). For Fyra’s purposes, identifying the right mix of suppliers per project type will be key – this is explored further in recommendations (Section F and G).

## **D. Compliance & Regulatory Deep-Dive**

Ensuring regulatory compliance is critical in hospitality projects. Hotels must meet strict fire safety, building codes, and durability standards. Reused products add complexity because documentation may not be readily available or original certifications may not cover current use. This section examines how operators address compliance requirements and what processes exist in Sweden for testing and certification of reused furnishings and materials in hospitality contexts.

### **Fire Safety Compliance (Brannteknisk compliance)**

Furniture & Textile Fire Standards: In hotels, upholstered furniture and fabrics must resist ignition. Key standards include EN 1021-1 and 1021-2 (cigarette and match test for furniture), often required for hotel room chairs/sofas. For public area furniture, Crib 5 (BS 5852 Source 5\) or stricter may be needed, equivalent to European classification EN 13501-1 for building materials. Curtains and draperies often need at least B-s1,d0 rating under EN 13501-1 (low flammability, low smoke) or local Swedish standards (SIS fire tests).

* Many reuse operators acknowledge this need: e.g., YLLW/Soeco has likely provided fire-safe fabric on reupholstered pieces to meet hospitality fire codes (they work with contract-grade textiles  
* [wihlborgs.se](https://www.wihlborgs.se/sv/om-oss/nyheter/hallbar-fastighetsutveckling/minskad-klimatpaverkan-med-aterbruk-av-kontorsmobler/#:~:text=,vd%20och%20grundare%20av%20Soeco)  
* ). Input explicitly tests and certifies when reusing for clients who require it  
* [greenified.se](https://greenified.se/#:~:text=Greenified%20%E2%80%93%20En%20cirkul%C3%A4r%20m%C3%B6belmarknad,ett%20kostnadseffektivt%20och%20h%C3%A5llbart%20s%C3%A4tt)  
* .

Certificates: Ideally, reused items come with original fire certification (e.g., a chair that was originally contract-grade will have manufacturer’s certs). Rekomo and RP often carry brands like Kinnarps or Herman Miller which were originally tested to EN standards – these certificates can be obtained from the manufacturer or public databases. Operators like Input can likely provide those original certificates for known products. If items lack certification (e.g., vintage pieces or custom-upholstered items), operators may:

* Arrange fire testing: Some have relationships with labs (RISE/SP in Sweden, or RISE – Research Institutes of Sweden is mentioned in context  
* [byggindustrin.se](https://www.byggindustrin.se/utveckling/hallbarhet/aterbruksexperter-om-marknadens-lycksokare-det-ar-rorigt-dar-ute-nu/#:~:text=%C3%85terbruksexperter%20om%20marknadens%20lycks%C3%B6kare%3A%20%E2%80%9DDet,i%20branschen%20kan%20leda)  
* ). They can send a sample of the reupholstered material for crib tests. This usually takes 2-4 weeks and costs a few thousand SEK per test. Cost allocation: Typically this cost might be borne by the client if it’s a special request, or split. Some operators might absorb it if it’s necessary for the project (especially larger contracts).  
* Offer fire-retardant treatment: There are professional services to treat fabrics or wood with flame retardants to achieve required class. For example, textile treatment with flame retardant can upgrade an item to Crib 5\. Operators like Kompanjonen or Input likely have partner contacts for this (they’ve been asked these questions by clients; a Q\&A from Avropa.se hints that such issues are known in industry)  
* [avropa.se](https://www.avropa.se/ramavtal/ramavtalsomraden/mobler-och-inredning/cirkulara-mobelfloden/cirkulara-mobelfloden-avyttring-av-mobler/#:~:text=inte%20ta%20med%20m%C3%B6bler%20i,m%C3%B6bler%3F%E2%80%9D%20under%20%E2%80%9DFr%C3%A5gor%20och%20svar%E2%80%9D)  
* [avropa.se](https://www.avropa.se/ramavtal/ramavtalsomraden/mobler-och-inredning/cirkulara-mobelfloden/cirkulara-mobelfloden-avyttring-av-mobler/#:~:text=F%C3%B6r%20s%C3%A5dana%20m%C3%B6bler%20d%C3%A4r%20man,inom%20Flyttj%C3%A4nster%20vara%20mer%20f%C3%B6rdelaktigt)  
* .  
  * The effect on appearance/haptics is usually minimal for fabrics (some slight stiffness or discoloration possible, which is why testing a sample is wise). Wood treatments can slightly dull the finish. Operators would communicate that trade-off.

Who handles and pays? Many reuse suppliers would say: we can facilitate it, but at extra cost and time. For a large hospitality order, they may bundle it in the contract. For instance, YLLW with a 3-year warranty may include ensuring compliance as part of quality guarantee (they want it “no compromise”

[yllw.com](https://www.yllw.com/yllw-factory#:~:text=Genom%20att%20k%C3%B6pa%20renoverad%20eller,v%C3%A4lja%20cirkul%C3%A4rt%20%E2%80%93%20utan%20kompromisser)

, which implies they’d ensure needed standards). Input likely adds a line item for any custom testing or treatment. Some, like RP or smaller ones, might rely on the client’s knowledge – if the client (Fyra/hotel) insists, they’ll comply but if not mentioned, they might skip it due to cost. Testing labs & process: In Sweden, RISE (formerly SP) is the main accredited lab for fire testing of furniture and materials. They can perform EN 1021 and BS 5852 tests fairly quickly. Some operators also use foreign labs (if faster or cheaper, e.g., UK or Danish labs). The typical timeline 2-4 weeks fits within project windows if planned early. It's recommended to test early (4+ months before use) to allow re-treatment or substitution if an item fails. Responsibility: Ideally, the reuse operator ensures compliance (especially Type A full-service ones). But for marketplace scenarios (Type D), the burden falls on the buyer. E.g., Place2Place simply sells items “as is” and disclaims such responsibilities; a hotel designer buying there must handle any necessary certification.

### **Building Material Compliance (Byggvarudeklarationer, BVD, etc.)**

BVD (Byggvarudeklaration): This is a Swedish building product declaration format that lists material contents, chemicals, etc., often used for green building rating or just to inform. For new building products, manufacturers provide BVD or similar Environmental Product Declarations (EPDs). For reused items, sometimes an “BVD Reuse” can be created stating the item is reused and referencing original materials if known.

* Operators providing BVD: Likely Återbruksfabriken and Kompanjonen being involved in CCBuild and green construction, can provide documentation. Återbruksfabriken explicitly mentions giving documentation to clients for environmental reporting  
* [aterbruksfabriken.se](https://aterbruksfabriken.se/#:~:text=Tj%C3%A4nster)  
* . This presumably includes material descriptions and possibly an EPD credit for reused vs new (they list CO2 savings on items  
* [aterbruksfabriken.se](https://aterbruksfabriken.se/#:~:text=1%20st%20Rund%20metallkanal%2C%20gr%C3%A5%2C,kg%20CO2%20Se%20alla%20produkter)  
* ).  
* Spirec and others might not have formal BVDs for each item (being a young startup), but as the market matures, more are using CCBuild’s standardized templates.

Chemical compliance (REACH, SVHC): Hotels and public buildings require assurance that furniture does not contain banned substances (like certain flame retardants, heavy metals, etc.). Reuse can be tricky because older items might have chemicals not allowed today. E.g., foam in chairs from 1990s might have PBDE flame retardants now banned. Operators’ approach:

* Intake screening: Reuse operators often avoid very old items that likely have harmful chemicals (like they might not accept 1980s foam seating because of this). Kompanjonen has noted lack of chemical content info as a barrier  
* [diva-portal.org](https://www.diva-portal.org/smash/get/diva2:1549635/FULLTEXT01.pdf#:~:text=subventioner%20bed%C3%B6ms%20dock%20som%20n%C3%B6dv%C3%A4ndiga,hos%20ink%C3%B6psorganisationer%20d%C3%A4r%20v%C3%A4rderingen%20av)  
* . Some use x-ray fluorescence (XRF) guns or rely on manufacturer info to check for things like brominated flame retardants.  
* Documentation: If they know the brand/model, they might obtain the materials list. Greenified by Input could supply original product environmental data if it exists.  
* If an item likely has hazardous content, reputable operators will not reuse it in sensitive environments. E.g., ceiling tiles with asbestos obviously discarded, or old PVC flooring with banned phthalates – not offered for reuse unless tested safe.  
* Kompanjonen and CCBuild encourage using a “logbook” approach to track materials in reuse  
* [diva-portal.org](https://www.diva-portal.org/smash/get/diva2:1549635/FULLTEXT01.pdf#:~:text=f%C3%B6r%20f%C3%B6rbr%C3%A4nning,Det)  
* .

VOC emissions: Hotels care about indoor air quality. Reused furniture typically has low VOC (any offgassing happened long ago), which is actually an advantage. But if pieces are refurbished (fresh paint, new foam), operators should use low-VOC finishes and certify that. Many reuse operators lean “eco” so they likely use water-based lacquers, OEKO-Tex textiles, etc. They can provide info on that on request. Allergens: For textiles (rugs, curtains), a concern is allergens or past contamination (mold, etc.). Reuse suppliers mitigate by professional cleaning and sometimes antimicrobial treatments. Few have formal allergen info, but they can declare if a used textile is wool (some people allergic) or if they applied any anti-dust-mite treatment.

### **Durability & Safety Standards for Hotel Furniture**

Hotels need contract-grade furniture:

* Chairs: EN 16139 for strength and durability (Level 1 or 2 depending on use area) is often required.  
* Tables: EN 1730 (stability, fatigue).  
* Beds: No single EN standard, but generally they should be robust for 24/7 use.  
* Reused contract furniture often already complies if originally made for commercial use. E.g., an ex-office chair usually meets EN 1335 or ANSI/BIFMA standards, which are acceptable.  
* The challenge is with residential-grade reused items making their way into hotels. E.g., a vintage home sofa might not withstand high usage. Thus:  
  * Top operators select only high-quality pieces (Soeco’s philosophy of using high-quality frames  
  * [wihlborgs.se](https://www.wihlborgs.se/sv/om-oss/nyheter/hallbar-fastighetsutveckling/minskad-klimatpaverkan-med-aterbruk-av-kontorsmobler/#:~:text=dem%20klassiska%20designm%C3%B6bler%20i%20behov,l%C3%A4gre%20pris%20och%20l%C3%A4gre%20klimatp%C3%A5verkan)  
  * ).  
  * They may do reinforcement during refurbishment (tighten joints, add brackets).  
  * Some might send a sample chair to a test lab for strength testing if it’s a big order of reused chairs.

Contract vs residential grade: Many operators mention they focus on high-quality brands (which often are contract grade even if used in offices). But if an operator offers a stylish older piece, one must consider if it was built for heavy use. Often, mid-century furniture was very well-built, but an operator like YLLW or RP might replace internal components (springs, support) to essentially bring it to modern contract robustness. They often give warranty (YLLW 3 years

[yllw.com](https://www.yllw.com/yllw-factory#:~:text=Vi%20renoverar%20och%20anpassar%20b%C3%A5de,l%C3%A4mnar%20minst%203%20%C3%A5rs%20garanti)

, RP 3 years

[rp.se](https://rp.se/#:~:text=,49)

) which indicates confidence in durability after their refurbishment. Maintenance & cleaning: Hotels require easily cleanable furnishings:

* Reused upholstery: Operators typically reupholster in contract fabrics which have high rub count and sometimes stain protection. Some even offer anti-microbial or easy-clean coatings on request (especially post-Covid, some offices asked for that too).  
* They provide cleaning instructions with the product info. E.g., if they supply second-hand armchairs, they’ll include info like “fabric is Trevira CS, washable at 60°C” or “leather – wipe with damp cloth”.  
* Casegoods: If they repaint furniture, they’ll use durable lacquer (possibly even marine-grade for high wear).  
* One could request an extra set of replaceable covers for reused chairs if they expect heavy wear; an operator like YLLW could sew spare covers from matching fabric as part of contract, given they handle upholstery.

### **Compliance Documentation Readiness**

We evaluate how prepared each operator is to deliver a “compliance packet” for hospitality projects:

* YLLW Factory: Ready (🟢) – They operate at a high professional level. While Soeco’s focus was offices, being a framework supplier to public entities means they’re used to providing documentation. They likely have a system for product descriptions with materials, certificates for any new fabrics used, and so on. Fire certs and test reports might be “Kan ordnas” (🟡) – if not already on file, they will arrange/obtain them on request. Given their stance of no compromise quality  
* [yllw.com](https://www.yllw.com/yllw-factory#:~:text=Genom%20att%20k%C3%B6pa%20renoverad%20eller,v%C3%A4lja%20cirkul%C3%A4rt%20%E2%80%93%20utan%20kompromisser)  
* , they would treat compliance seriously, even if it means extra steps.  
* Input/Greenified: Very Ready (🟢) – Input has a compliance team. They can produce fire certificates, EN test references, material declarations, likely even EPD data for reused content (they participated in projects calculating 195 ton CO2 savings  
* [eon.se](https://www.eon.se/content/dam/eon-se/swe-documents/swe-sammanfattning-analys-kontorsflytt-2023.pdf#:~:text=,akt%C3%B6rer%20inte%20ska%20fokusera%20p%C3%A5)  
* , implying they do custom sustainability reporting). They probably have templates ready to fill for each reused item (detailing original manufacturer, year, etc., which covers provenance and potentially previous use context).  
* Rekomo: Moderate (🟡) – They have ISO certifications and work with many municipalities (they display Kammarkollegiet contract symbol  
* [rekomo.se](https://www.rekomo.se/?srsltid=AfmBOoqU2ohc2dmqC6KgIBjGOZ1HrsNQYeJkBlUgsBpSFgQwbDEEUow7#:~:text=Image%3A%20iso%20certificate%20%20,162)  
* , suggesting they are an approved supplier perhaps). So they likely can provide basic info (brand, model of furniture, maybe flame resistance if known). But they don’t routinely supply hotels, so things like a full BVB or REACH statement might not be at hand unless asked. They might scramble a bit if a client asks for, say, an EPD for each reused desk. They can give qualitative info (“used item, already amortized environmental footprint”) but less formal documentation.  
* RP: Moderate (🟡) – They have a sustainability report and clearly advertise their policies  
* [rp.se](https://rp.se/#:~:text=,49)  
* [rp.se](https://rp.se/#:~:text=P%C3%A5%20RP%20erbjuder%20vi%20mer,b%C3%A5de%20milj%C3%B6v%C3%A4nlig%20och%20ekonomiskt%20f%C3%B6rdelaktig)  
* . For compliance, they likely provide warranty docs (3-year functional guarantee), and any requested test info they have. Fire certification would be new to them for hospitality – they’d need to partner up for that (so they can but haven’t done it frequently – call that 🟡 can be arranged).  
* Kompanjonen: High for consulting (🟢) – They can compile detailed documentation because their value to corporate clients is partly in reporting. They can produce a “reuse report” with all items, their origin, age, and any known compliance info. They know where to get missing pieces (lab tests, etc.). They did mention compliance is often a perceived barrier and they work to solve it  
* [byggindustrin.se](https://www.byggindustrin.se/utveckling/hallbarhet/aterbruksexperter-om-marknadens-lycksokare-det-ar-rorigt-dar-ute-nu/#:~:text=%C3%85terbruksexperter%20om%20marknadens%20lycks%C3%B6kare%3A%20%E2%80%9DDet,i%20branschen%20kan%20leda)  
* . Not being a manufacturer, they won’t have ready-made certs, but they will ensure they are obtained.  
* Återbruksfabriken: Ready (🟢) – For building materials, they absolutely provide documentation. They likely have BVDs for each product entry or at least a statement of material type and condition. They also provide CO2 savings data  
* [aterbruksfabriken.se](https://aterbruksfabriken.se/#:~:text=1%20st%20Rund%20metallkanal%2C%20gr%C3%A5%2C,kg%20CO2%20Se%20alla%20produkter)  
*  and likely any lab results if they test structural components. They aim to support future building code compliance with reused materials  
* [aterbruksfabriken.se](https://aterbruksfabriken.se/#:~:text=P%C3%A5%20kort%20sikt%20fokuserar%20vi,att%20g%C3%A5%20direkt%20till%20deponi)  
* , so documentation is part of their service.  
* Others (Place2Place, etc.): Minimal (🔴) – They operate more like classifieds, so they provide item description but not formal compliance docs. The burden is on buyer to vet.

Preparation time: For well-prepared operators (Input, YLLW), they could assemble the needed documentation within a couple of weeks, since they have much info in databases (YLLW’s database tracks condition and presumably product details

[wihlborgs.se](https://www.wihlborgs.se/sv/om-oss/nyheter/hallbar-fastighetsutveckling/minskad-klimatpaverkan-med-aterbruk-av-kontorsmobler/#:~:text=Soeco%20har%20kontor%2C%20utst%C3%A4llning%20och,f%C3%A5r%20%C3%B6nskat%20utseende%20och%20funktion)

). For others, it might take longer and some pushing. E.g., a smaller supplier might need 4-6 weeks to get a fire test done and to gather all tech specs. Extra costs: Expect additional fees for:

* Fire testing or treatment (often a few thousand SEK per item type).  
* Structural engineering approvals for reused materials if needed.  
* Extended warranties or certifications beyond standard. Some operators might treat it as part of big project overhead, others will line-item it.

Summary: Ensuring reused items meet hospitality codes is definitely doable in Sweden’s context, but it requires early planning (test and treat well in advance of installation) and often close collaboration between the design team and the reuse supplier. The top-tier operators are increasingly aware of these needs and either have internal solutions or partner networks to address them. Hotels aiming for green building certifications will also require data on reused content (like % reused vs new, carbon savings) – many operators can provide those metrics now (e.g., CCBuild marketplace accumulative data, YLLW citing saved emissions

[yllw.com](https://www.yllw.com/yllw-factory#:~:text=65000)

). So with the right operators, compliance and documentation, while a challenge, can be managed so that reused furnishings are delivered “up to code” and with clear provenance and environmental impact data.

## **E. Markedsanalyse – Svensk gjenbrukslandskap (Market Analysis of the Swedish Reuse Landscape)**

This section provides a broader market analysis of the B2B reuse industry in Sweden, especially as it relates to furniture and building materials supply for projects. We look at market size, structure, key segments, value chain, trends, and a brief comparison with Finland’s reuse market.

### **Market Size & Structure**

The Swedish B2B reuse market for furniture and interiors is growing but still emerging towards maturity:

* Number of operators: Estimated on the order of dozens of significant B2B-focused reuse operators. A rough breakdown:  
  * Perhaps 5–10 larger companies dominate office furniture reuse (e.g., Rekomo, YLLW, RP, Input/Greenified, DPJ’s used division).  
  * Another 5–10 specialized players in building materials reuse (Återbruksfabriken, Kompanjonen, Brattöns, Spirec, etc.).  
  * Several digital platforms (CCBuild, Loopfront, etc.) acting as aggregators.  
  * Then many smaller local stores or non-profits that occasionally do B2B but primarily handle consumer or small-scale reuse (e.g., municipal second-hand depots, social enterprises like “Återbruket” in various towns).

Market concentration: It’s relatively fragmented but with a few front-runners:

* Office furniture reuse has a couple of big players (Rekomo, YLLW) that operate across regions, implying moderate concentration in that sub-segment.  
* Building material reuse is currently fragmented regionally (e.g., one hub in Stockholm, one in Gothenburg, etc.) – but efforts like CCBuild aim to connect them, not necessarily consolidate but at least network them.  
* There is little direct competition yet in hospitality reuse specifically – whoever has done hotels likely did so as one-off or from office segment pivot. So no single company monopolizes “hotel reuse supply”.

Geographical distribution:

* Stockholm – a hotbed for reuse startups and initiatives (because largest market of offices and construction). Kompanjonen, Input’s HQ, and the “Återbrukshubben” pilot in Solna suggest concentration. The city also pushes circular economy in procurement.  
* Gothenburg – also active: e.g., Brattöns, Rekomo has large presence, and likely some circular projects via Chalmers and Riksbyggen. West Sweden has heavy industry that might feed materials into reuse.  
* Skåne (Malmö/Lund) – very engaged in sustainability: Malmö has had Återbyggdepå for \~20 years  
* [facebook.com](https://www.facebook.com/smalmo/videos/andreas-sch%C3%B6nstr%C3%B6m-s-ber%C3%A4ttar-om-malm%C3%B6-%C3%A5terbyggdep%C3%A5/220902238979850/#:~:text=Andreas%20Sch%C3%B6nstr%C3%B6m%20,sedan%2020%20%C3%A5r%20Malm%C3%B6)  
* , Wihlborgs (a big real estate firm in Malmö) embraced reuse with Soeco/YLLW  
* [wihlborgs.se](https://www.wihlborgs.se/sv/om-oss/nyheter/hallbar-fastighetsutveckling/minskad-klimatpaverkan-med-aterbruk-av-kontorsmobler/#:~:text=P%C3%A5%20Soeco%20f%C3%A5r%20gamla%20Andy,%C3%A5terbruk%20med%20fokus%20p%C3%A5%20kontorsinredning)  
* [wihlborgs.se](https://www.wihlborgs.se/sv/om-oss/nyheter/hallbar-fastighetsutveckling/minskad-klimatpaverkan-med-aterbruk-av-kontorsmobler/#:~:text=Ramavtalet%20som%20Wihlborgs%20har%20med,avkall%20p%C3%A5%20funktion%20eller%20design)  
* . YLLW (Soeco) itself is based there. So Skåne has both private and public emphasis on reuse.  
* Northern Sweden: Less representation. Possibly because fewer large offices to generate supply and less local demand. Some initiatives might exist (like recycling centers repurposing building parts, but low commercial scale yet). So the market is mostly south-central Sweden.

Growth rate: The market is growing quickly:

* Many companies (like Soeco/YLLW) reported \~30% annual growth  
* [wihlborgs.se](https://www.wihlborgs.se/sv/om-oss/nyheter/hallbar-fastighetsutveckling/minskad-klimatpaverkan-med-aterbruk-av-kontorsmobler/#:~:text=Infl%C3%B6det%20%C3%A4r%20s%C3%A5%20stort%20att,i%20snitt%2C%20%C3%A4r%20ett%20annat)  
* .  
* New entrants like Återbruksfabriken (2021), Spirec (2022) show increase in venture activity.  
* The government and big firms (Folksam, Vasakronan, etc.) are supporting reuse pilots, indicating expansion.  
* Perhaps 2-3 significant new entrants per year in various niches (some may stay small, others scaling).

### **Segment Analysis by Product Category**

Furniture (office/hospitality furniture):

* Maturity: Among reuse segments, furniture is relatively mature in Sweden. Office furniture reuse has been around for decades (e.g., Vican since 1989, Rekomo \~30 years). This segment benefitted early from cost savings drivers and abundant supply from office churn.  
* Competitive intensity: Medium but increasing – traditional dealers now have to consider circular offerings (like Input did with Greenified, DPJ started used section), showing competition. Still, not all mainstream have caught up, so players like Rekomo grew in the absence of too many competitors, but now with YLLW’s rise and others, competition intensifies.  
* Best players: YLLW/Soeco, Rekomo, RP, Input/Greenified – these lead in volume and capability. Niche quality player includes VarUnik (if focusing on hotels, though that’s more service model).  
* Gaps: Perhaps less coverage on residential furniture in B2B context, but that’s outside our scope. For hospitality, a gap might be an operator specializing exclusively in hotel furniture reuse (one that handles decorative and casegoods systematically) – currently not clearly present; office-focused players adapt to it.

Textiles:

* Maturity: This is an emerging sub-segment. Historically, second-hand textiles in B2B were rare due to laundry/hygiene and certification (flame retardancy) issues. But now:  
  * Efforts like VarUnik’s hotel textile subscription (just launched in 2022\) show innovation – presumably they provide linens made of recycled fibers or circulate linens between hotels as a service  
  * [ca.se](https://ca.se/category/b2b/#:~:text=Ny%20cirkul%C3%A4r%20abonnemangstj%C3%A4nst%20f%C3%B6r%20hotell)  
  * .  
  * Siptex – an automated textile sorting facility by Sysav (Skåne)  
  * [ca.se](https://ca.se/category/b2b/#:~:text=Siptex%20vinnare%20av%20Stockholm%20Fashion,Districts%20h%C3%A5llbarhetspris%20Encouragement%20for%20Action)  
  *  is an upstream enabler (sorts textiles for recycling but also possibly identification for reuse).  
* Niche players: not many specifically for reused textiles. Matting AB maybe does reuse of carpet tiles?  
* Gaps: Hotels need FR-rated curtains, etc. There might be companies reconditioning theater curtains or similar, but it’s niche. It's likely an area for growth – e.g., a service to clean and certify used hotel curtains could develop.

Building Materials:

* Maturity: Still early but rapidly developing. The construction sector only recently embraced reuse at scale. Initiatives like CCBuild (coordinated by IVL) indicate a push towards industrial scale reuse  
* [ivl.se](https://www.ivl.se/vart-erbjudande/forskning/cirkulara-floden/cirkulara-produktfloden-i-byggsektorn.html#:~:text=Centrum%20f%C3%B6r%20cirkul%C3%A4rt%20byggande%20,p%C3%A5%20systemniv%C3%A5%20mot%20cirkul%C3%A4ra)  
* .  
* Key players: Kompanjonen (10 years in building material reuse  
* [diva-portal.org](https://www.diva-portal.org/smash/get/diva2:1549635/FULLTEXT01.pdf#:~:text=3,att%20koppla%20ihop%20tillg%C3%A5ng%20och)  
* , an early mover), Brattöns (over 10 years, essentially a salvage yard veteran  
* [kommunochnaringsliv.se](https://kommunochnaringsliv.se/redan-en-veteran-inom-aterbruk/#:~:text=N%C3%A4ringsliv%20kommunochnaringsliv,F%C3%B6retaget%20demonterar%20byggmaterial%20som)  
* ), Malmö Återbyggdepå (a long-running depot), Wiklunds (a demolition/logistics firm pivoting to reuse partner  
* [wiklunds.se](https://www.wiklunds.se/affarsomraden/cirkulart-byggande/#:~:text=Wiklunds%20kan%20vara%20byggherrens%20samarbetspartner,%C3%A4r%20att%20skapa%20goda)  
* ), Ragn-Sells (big waste co, piloting reuse with others  
* [shiftsweden.se](https://www.shiftsweden.se/Media/sg2bfjo0/utvecklad-marknad-och-logistik-for-aterbruk.pdf#:~:text=ShiftSweden%20www.shiftsweden.se%20%20Ragn,Lindholmen%20Science%20Park%2C%20Lunds)  
* ).  
* Emerging segment: yes, many pilots – e.g., large developers like Fabege setting up reuse warehouses on their dev sites  
* [svenskttra.se](https://www.svenskttra.se/om-oss/aktuellt/2024/2/vilka-ar-de-storsta-utmaningarna-med-att-aterbruka-byggmaterial/#:~:text=Vilka%20%C3%A4r%20de%20st%C3%B6rsta%20utmaningarna,har%20man%20nu%20%C3%B6ppnat%20%C3%85terbrukshubben)  
* .  
* Key players: Also mention of “Återbrukshubben” by Fabege, and “Loop Rocks” by NCC (for rubble etc.).  
* Challenges: Regulatory barriers (product liability for structural items, lack of standards), logistical costs for heavy materials, and matching supply-demand timing.  
* But momentum is strong due to climate goals. Likely significant growth in next 5 years as new building regulations might mandate reuse consideration.

Lighting:

* Maturity: Moderate. Office lighting (ceiling fixtures, task lamps) often gets reused moderately (Rekomo, etc., do sell some used lamps). Some specialist may exist: e.g., LightReuse (not sure if an actual company, hypothetical).  
* Challenges: Rapid tech change (LED efficiency improving means older fixtures may be less desired; also connected/smart lighting preferences). But high-end decorative lighting can be very worthwhile to reuse (vintage designer lamps are valued).  
* Players: No dedicated ones identified, but general furniture resellers handle some. Also, demolition sales often include large lots of light fixtures (Kompanjonen and CCBuild have these listings).  
* Gaps: Possibly a business opportunity for a lighting-specific refurbisher (e.g., rewiring and certifying used lamps for new projects).  
* For hospitality, a lot of hotels toss lamps during reno – few systematically reuse them. A notable gap: an operator offering refurbished hotel guestroom lamps with new wiring and shades to meet standards could find market, but currently hotels likely buy new due to code concerns.

Dedicated Hotel Reuse Operators:

* There aren’t many dedicated hospitality reuse suppliers yet. VarUnik’s new service is one example focusing on hotel textiles. We haven’t seen “Hotel surplus outlet AB” – but in other countries (like the US) companies buy bulk furniture from hotels and resell to smaller hotels or B2C. In Sweden, this might happen ad-hoc (auction houses handling hotel liquidations, e.g., PS Auction sometimes sells entire hotel contents).  
* It seems Sweden does not yet have a big company that markets specifically to hotels for reused FF\&E (Furniture, Fixtures & Equipment). This is likely because historically hotels prefer brand new for image reasons, but that is now changing with sustainability trends. So we might foresee one of the office reuse companies launching a “hospitality division” soon.

### **Value Chain Mapping**

Understanding the flow of goods in the reuse ecosystem: Supply sources (input of used items):

* Office closures and corporate refurbishments – major source of quality furniture (desks, chairs, cabinets). E.g., when a bank redecorates, hundreds of items enter the reuse stream (via auctions or companies like Kompanjonen, Rekomo).  
* Hotel renovations/closures – potentially large source of furniture, carpets, light fixtures, even bathroom fixtures. Historically, much was scrapped or sold overseas. Now, if harnessed, it’s a goldmine for reuse: e.g., 300 hotel rooms worth of furniture can supply multiple smaller projects. However, currently, specialized handling is lacking (some furniture might go to staff or local auctions).  
* Public sector – municipalities and government agencies often have surplus furniture (schools, hospitals etc.), which feed into reuse operators or auction sites. Also, old building materials from public demolitions can be funneled (some municipalities have internal reuse hubs).  
* Demolition contractors – key players like Rivners, Skanska etc. If they partner with reuse hubs, valuable materials (doors, raised floors, HVAC equipment) can be rescued instead of scrapped. E.g., Wiklunds (a demolition/logistics firm) now offers to salvage and store materials for reuse  
* [wiklunds.se](https://www.wiklunds.se/affarsomraden/cirkulart-byggande/#:~:text=Wiklunds%20kan%20vara%20byggherrens%20samarbetspartner,%C3%A4r%20att%20skapa%20goda)  
* .  
* Manufacturers’ surplus – sometimes new products that are overstock, returns, or out-of-production stock enter the reuse market. E.g., Spirec buying unused trim boards that were mis-ordered  
* [spirec.se](https://spirec.se/pages/om-spirec?srsltid=AfmBOoqLO7_aAICcobHJfkiy90CEch2sYI1sSnIdrfic389VPTe1cPfM#:~:text=Det%20kan%20%C3%A4ven%20vara%20helt,formvirke%20som%20anv%C3%A4nts%20vid%20gjutning)  
* . Also, furniture makers may resell returned items via second-hand partners.  
* Bankruptcy liquidations – unfortunately a source: failed businesses (restaurants, stores) whose contents get sold off (often via auction, but sometimes reuse companies step in).  
* Consumers? – Generally, B2B channels don’t rely on individual consumers’ used goods (except maybe antiques or unique pieces from estates that could be repurposed in commercial projects by designers).

Processing / refurbishment:

* Refurbishment workshops – either in-house (Yllw’s big factory  
* [tema.storynews.se](https://tema.storynews.se/soeco/#:~:text=Agendadrivna%20%C3%A5terbruksf%C3%B6retaget%20Soeco%20s%C3%A4ljer%20begagnade,att%20g%C3%B6ra%20avkall%20p%C3%A5)  
* , RP’s “möbelrekond”) or subcontracted (e.g., Artex does upholstery for many reuse firms  
* [artex.se](https://www.artex.se/sv/referenser/hyfer-objects#:~:text=RP%20M%C3%96BLER%20OCH%20INREDNING%20,Vi%20renoverar%20m%C3%B6blerna)  
* ). They clean, repair, reupholster, refinish. This stage adds value by ensuring items are project-ready, safe, and aesthetically suitable.  
* Testing labs – part of processing if needed for compliance (e.g., RISE doing fire tests, as mentioned). Not continuous flow, but an important supporting actor.  
* Cleaning and certification – for materials like wood beams, maybe structural engineers check them. For textiles, industrial laundries (if someone reused acoustic ceiling panels, for instance, they'd need cleaning).  
* Customization – some players do modifications (cutting to size, painting a uniform color scheme). There are carpentry shops specialized in adjusting standard modules (e.g., cutting down a large table to smaller ones).

Distribution:

* Logistics specialists – Large furniture reuse needs big transport and often assembly. Some reuse firms have their own trucks, others use logistics companies experienced in handling bulky items (like MTAB for fine art and furniture, or regular freight).  
* Warehousing – Storage in between is crucial, especially to accumulate enough identical items or to hold them until a project needs them (the “inventory waiting for use” time). Big players have warehouses (5000 m² for Återbruksfabriken  
* [aterbruksfabriken.se](https://aterbruksfabriken.se/#:~:text=5000%20m%C2%B2%20lager%20och%20AI,teknik%20f%C3%B6rvandlar%20rester%20till%20resurser)  
* , 2500 m² showroom for Kontorsmiljö in Linköping  
* [kontorsmiljo.com](https://www.kontorsmiljo.com/affarsomraden/aterbruk/#:~:text=%C3%85terbruk%20,Allt%20fr%C3%A5n%20Malmstolen%2C%20Officeline%2C)  
* , etc.). There are also external warehouse providers that might partner to store reclaimed materials near cities.  
* Digital platforms – Part of distribution nowadays: they list what’s available and connect distant supply & demand. CCBuild’s marketplace, Blocket (Swedish classifieds) which sometimes used by businesses, and specialized auctions (e.g., PS Auction, Klaravik) play a role in transacting used business inventory.

### **Ecosystem and Infrastructure**

Enabling Infrastructure:

* Testing Labs: RISE is central. Also Swedish Technical Research Institute if separate, and possibly others (universities doing tests as part of research projects).  
* Refurbishment Services: Besides the reuse companies themselves, there are independent ones: e.g., Artex AB (mentioned in search as a partner to RP  
* [artex.se](https://www.artex.se/sv/referenser/hyfer-objects#:~:text=RP%20M%C3%96BLER%20OCH%20INREDNING%20,Vi%20renoverar%20m%C3%B6blerna)  
* ) focuses on reupholstering and renovating furniture. Tapetserarverkstäder (traditional upholstery studios) also sometimes collaborate on high-end projects. Carpentry shops that take on re-finishing wood furniture. These service providers are crucial to scale quality refurbishment.  
* Logistics Specialists: Companies like Wiklunds pivoting to reuse means they provide both removal and delivery services. Also standard freight companies adapt to handling not just palletized new goods but odd used items (some differences in packing).  
* Inventory Management Systems: CCBuild provides an online platform for tracking an organization’s reusable assets (like a database so companies can post internally first). Some large property owners build their own reuse inventory systems for their portfolio, then link with external marketplaces.  
* Marketplaces: Apart from CCBuild, there's Byggnetto or Byggigen (older marketplace for building goods), and broad ones like Blocket or Facebook Marketplace (not specialized, but definitely used by small businesses to sell furniture).

Policy & Support:

* The Swedish government and agencies are pushing circular economy:  
  * Public procurement guidelines now encourage reuse. There are framework agreements e.g., “Cirkulära Möbelflöden” (circular furniture flows) by Kammarkollegiet  
  * [avropa.se](https://www.avropa.se/Nyheter/2025/forvaltningen/soeco-kontorsmobler-ab-namnandrar/#:~:text=Publicerad%202025)  
  * . So agencies can call off reuse services from approved vendors (Soeco/YLLW was one such vendor).  
  * Subsidies/Grants: Vinnova (Sweden’s innovation agency) has funded circular business model research and pilots  
  * [diva-portal.org](https://www.diva-portal.org/smash/get/diva2:1446500/FULLTEXT01.pdf#:~:text=,och%20utveckling%20av%20effektiva%20innovationssystem)  
  * . Naturvårdsverket (Environmental agency) as well  
  * [naturvardsverket.se](https://www.naturvardsverket.se/496a67/globalassets/media/publikationer-pdf/7100/978-91-620-7163-9.pdf?utm_source=chatgpt.com#:~:text=,QUEST%29%2C)  
  * .  
  * Regulation changes: Starting 2022, changes in waste legislation require sorting out construction waste for reuse, making it easier for reuse operators to get materials instead of them being downcycled.  
  * Potential upcoming requirement: new buildings may get mandatory reuse quotas or at least documentation of efforts (discussions ongoing).  
* Barriers: Some regulations ironically hinder reuse:  
  * Fire regulations may force testing that is costly, deterring reuse if budget doesn’t allow (why some hotels just buy new with certificates).  
  * Warranty/liability concerns: New products come with long warranties; for reused, who is liable if a reused item fails? Operators mitigate with their own warranties (e.g., YLLW’s 3-year guarantee  
  * [yllw.com](https://www.yllw.com/yllw-factory#:~:text=Vi%20renoverar%20och%20anpassar%20b%C3%A5de,l%C3%A4mnar%20minst%203%20%C3%A5rs%20garanti)  
  *  helps).  
  * Procurement rules: Public bodies sometimes found it tricky to include used options in tenders due to specification habits or fear of inconsistent supply. This is improving with new frameworks and knowledge sharing.  
* Support programs: There are networks like CCBuild (supported by government, IVL  
* [vinnova.se](https://www.vinnova.se/p/centrum-for-cirkulart-byggande--samverkan-for-aterbruk-och-cirkulara-materialfloden-i-bygg--och-fastighetssektorn/#:~:text=,byggnation%2C%20rivning%20och%20f%C3%B6)  
* ) that provide knowledge and even matchmaking events. The emergence of “Återbruk inom bygg” conferences or Green Building Council events on reuse indicates knowledge dissemination as a support.

### **Trend Signals**

Drivers of Growth:

* Sustainability/Climate Awareness: Companies and hotel chains have set ESG (Environmental, Social, Governance) targets including waste reduction and lower carbon footprint. Reuse of furniture directly cuts carbon (e.g., reusing one workstation can save tens of kg CO₂  
* [aterbruksfabriken.se](https://aterbruksfabriken.se/#:~:text=1%20st%20Rund%20metallkanal%2C%20gr%C3%A5%2C,kg%20CO2%20Se%20alla%20produkter)  
* ). As such, reuse aligns with science-based targets many firms adopt.  
* ESG Reporting Requirements: EU regulations (like CSRD) will force reporting of scope 3 emissions – using reused furniture can reduce embodied carbon of fit-outs, so businesses see that as a metric to report positively on. Already Wihlborgs (property company) did a study showing tenants are open to reuse  
* [wihlborgs.se](https://www.wihlborgs.se/sv/om-oss/nyheter/hallbar-fastighetsutveckling/minskad-klimatpaverkan-med-aterbruk-av-kontorsmobler/#:~:text=En%20studie%20baserad%20p%C3%A5%2060,i%20framtiden%20anv%C3%A4nda%20%C3%A5terbrukade%20produkter)  
* .  
* Cost Savings in Uncertain Economy: With economic uncertainties (post-pandemic, inflation), businesses and hotel owners look to cut costs. Reused goods at 50% off new provide substantial savings for renovation budgets  
* [rekomo.se](https://www.rekomo.se/?srsltid=AfmBOoqU2ohc2dmqC6KgIBjGOZ1HrsNQYeJkBlUgsBpSFgQwbDEEUow7#:~:text=Vi%20k%C3%B6per%2C%20s%C3%A4ljer%20och%20hyr,Och%20f%C3%B6r%20din%20budget)  
* . In downturns, reuse tends to get a boost as a cost-saving measure.  
* Circular Economy Hype ("Circular chic"): Reuse is also getting a trendy image – designers incorporate vintage for that unique character (especially in boutique hospitality, this is now a design trend that also tells a story to guests). It’s become “chic” to say furnishings are upcycled or have history.  
* Regulatory Nudges: as mentioned, public sector leading by example (frame agreements, etc.) and potential building code changes (the government’s strategy for circular economy pushing construction sector to reuse more by 2025 and beyond).  
* Innovation & Tech: better digital tools (AI-driven inventory scanning  
* [aterbruksfabriken.se](https://aterbruksfabriken.se/#:~:text=Image%3A%20%C3%A5terbruk%2C%20utan%20omv%C3%A4gar)  
* , blockchain traceability for materials, etc.) are reducing friction in finding and trusting reused items.

Challenges:

* Quality Perception: There is still a mindset in parts of hospitality that “used \= inferior”. Luxury segment has begun to overcome this by reframing reused as “authentic” or “vintage”. But mid-market chain hotels fear guest perceptions; they need assurance that reused means *as good as new* in comfort and cleanliness. This is why refurbishment quality and marketing (storytelling about sustainability) are key.  
* Logistics Complexity: Reuse supply chains are not as streamlined as new. Instead of ordering 100 identical chairs from a manufacturer’s catalog (made on schedule), you may have to collect those 100 chairs from 3 different sources, refurbish, store, and then deliver just in time. This is inherently more complex. It requires better planning and sometimes higher transaction costs. Many smaller reuse shops don’t have capacity to handle that complexity, which can deter large customers.  
* Compliance Burden: As discussed, proving compliance (fire, etc.) can be more work for reused items. Until there’s a universally accepted process (like maybe a certification label for “Refurbished & Compliant” furniture), each project requires case-by-case effort, which can scare off project managers.  
* Fragmented Supply: It can be hard to guarantee consistent supply for repetitive needs. For example, a hotel chain wanting to roll out reused furniture across 10 hotels would worry if enough similar stock can be found for all properties. Without central coordination, supply is hit or miss. This fragmentation makes it tough to plan reuse at scale (though platforms like CCBuild are trying to aggregate supply).  
* Data Gaps: Lack of product data on old items (material composition, fire rating, etc.) as noted in studies  
* [diva-portal.org](https://www.diva-portal.org/smash/get/diva2:1549635/FULLTEXT01.pdf#:~:text=subventioner%20bed%C3%B6ms%20dock%20som%20n%C3%B6dv%C3%A4ndiga,hos%20ink%C3%B6psorganisationer%20d%C3%A4r%20v%C3%A4rderingen%20av)  
* makes it harder to confidently reuse them. Efforts to implement Material Passports for new products aim to solve this for future reuse – a trend to watch.

Innovations:

* Digital Twins / BIM integration: There’s exploration of linking building info models (BIM) with reuse inventory. E.g., having a digital model of a used item that designers can drop into their plans (facilitating “designing with reuse”).  
* AI-driven matching: Some startups use AI to match posted surplus items with requests (like how Återbruksfabriken claims AI in inventory sorting  
* [aterbruksfabriken.se](https://aterbruksfabriken.se/#:~:text=AI)  
* ). In future, an AI might quickly tell you “X warehouse has 50 chairs that fit your design spec” by analyzing images and metadata.  
* Blockchain for traceability: to certify provenance and perhaps to streamline ownership transfer and liability tracking of reused products. If a reused item had a blockchain record, it might include past fire certs, refurb details, giving buyers confidence.  
* Leasing/Subscription models: Instead of selling reused furniture, companies like VarUnik propose leasing it to hotels (so hotels can frequently refresh style in a circular way). This model, if it takes off, ensures products are not discarded but rotated. It also transfers maintenance duty to the provider (like a furniture-as-a-service).  
* Standardization efforts: The industry might develop standardized grades or labels for reused items (“Grade A refurbished – meets XYZ standards”), making procurement easier for clients. Also, possibly modular designs enabling easy refurb (though that’s more on new product design side feeding reuse).

### **Finland vs. Sweden Comparison**

Finland, being another Nordic country with strong sustainability ethos, provides a useful comparison:

* Market Maturity: Finland’s reuse market has also grown, especially with initiatives like Motto (Motiva’s circular procurement guide) and companies like Kierrätyskeskus (Reuse centers) and some active in offices (there’s a notable one, maybe Martela’s Martela Outlet selling used office furniture). However, Sweden appears to have more specialized private companies scaling up (like the ones we listed). Finland’s market might be slightly behind in terms of number of players and scale, partly due to smaller population and a bit less office stock.  
* Cross-border usage: Could Fyra use Finnish suppliers? Possibly in some cases:  
  * Martela (a Finnish furniture manufacturer) runs a circular service called Martela Lifecycle that refurbishes their furniture for clients – they could potentially supply Swedish projects if needed, but they mainly focus domestic.  
  * Some Finnish architecture firms incorporate reclaimed materials; there may be salvage yards (e.g., Rakennusapteekki in Finland for old house parts, though more B2C).  
  * Loopfront originally started in Norway/Finland, and expanded to Sweden – they might have a cross-border digital platform.  
  * Practically, moving bulky used goods internationally only makes sense for unique high-value items or if local supply is insufficient. For northern Sweden, sourcing from Finland (Oulu region) might make sense for some materials.  
* Cross-border operators: Input Interiör operates in Finland as well (they have presence in Helsinki), so Greenified concept may cover Finland soon if not already. Also, Kinnarps (Swedish manufacturer) has a Finnish branch and they started offering take-back of furniture; those channels might operate across Nordics.  
* What Sweden can learn from Finland: Finland has been strong in public-private collaboration on circular pilots. For example, the city of Helsinki did circular furniture procurement for its offices early on. Also, Finnish innovation like Maalaamo (Paintshop) – refurbishing old school furniture with kids or such social enterprise models might inspire Sweden.  
* What Finland can learn from Sweden: Sweden’s development of networks (like CCBuild) and integrating reuse into public frameworks is a model Finland might emulate. The scale of companies like YLLW or Rekomo is not yet seen in Finland – so Finland could import those business models or even see these companies expand there.  
* It's noted that both countries share Nordic environmental goals, so knowledge is likely exchanged. Some Swedish operators might already serve Finland (Rekomo said “whole Nordics”  
* [rekomo.se](https://www.rekomo.se/?srsltid=AfmBOoqU2ohc2dmqC6KgIBjGOZ1HrsNQYeJkBlUgsBpSFgQwbDEEUow7#:~:text=Vi%20k%C3%B6per%2C%20s%C3%A4ljer%20och%20hyr,Och%20f%C3%B6r%20din%20budget)  
* ). Cross-border logistics cost may limit but not prohibit – e.g., shipping a truck of furniture from Stockholm to Helsinki is feasible for a big project if the price is right.

In summary, Sweden’s reuse landscape in 2025 is dynamic: gaining momentum, diversifying across categories, and becoming more professionalized. While still fragmented and facing some barriers, the combination of environmental urgency, economic incentives, and innovative solutions is accelerating the shift from niche to mainstream. Hospitality is poised to be one of the next frontiers, taking cues from the office segment’s success and adapting to its own demands.

## **F. Shortlist & Rekommandasjoner (Shortlist & Recommendations)**

Based on the detailed operator profiles and market analysis above, we present a shortlist of 12 top operators segmented into tiers. These tiers reflect their readiness and suitability for Fyra’s typical hospitality projects, particularly focusing on volume capability, compliance, and track record: TIER 1: Hospitality-Ready, Full-Service Hubs (Top 3-4)

These are operators that meet almost all critical requirements – volume, quality, compliance, and hospitality experience – making them suitable for immediate engagement on hotel projects.

1. YLLW Factory (formerly Soeco) – *Full-service reuse furniture hub with hospitality focus.*  
   * Core: Offers complete circular furniture solutions (large stock, custom refurb, logistics). Headquarters in Dalby (Skåne) with operations covering all Sweden and Nordics. \~40 staff, established 2011\.

     * In-house refurbishment factory – high quality (3-year warranty on all items)

     * .

How is pricing structured for reused items vs your new catalog?”

3. Rekomo AB – *Leading supplier of used office furniture (Nordic).*  
   * Core: Buys/resells used office furniture at scale. Based in Gothenburg (with large warehouses in Stockholm, Malmö). \~50 employees.  
   * Hospitality Experience: Limited direct, but they did furnish e.g., Liseberg offices rapidly  
   * [rekomo.se](https://www.rekomo.se/?srsltid=AfmBOoqU2ohc2dmqC6KgIBjGOZ1HrsNQYeJkBlUgsBpSFgQwbDEEUow7#:~:text=Tre%20popul%C3%A4ra%20artiklar%20%2012,Nyfiken%3F%20%208205%2C8299%20%201328515%2C8303%2C8299)  
   *  and do café furniture which overlaps with casual hospitality. Possibly supplied furniture to co-living or student hotels which are similar to hotel setup. Not many explicit hotel references but capabilities overlap strongly with mid-market hotel needs (beds aside).  
   * Strengths:  
     * Huge ready stock for immediate needs (fast turnaround, “snabb leverans” is their selling point  
     * [sono.se](https://www.sono.se/aterbruk/kontor.html#:~:text=V%C3%A5ra%20%C3%A5terbrukade%20m%C3%B6bler%20har%20h%C3%B6g,p%C3%A5%20v%C3%A5ra%20second%20han%20kontorsm%C3%B6bler)  
     * ).  
     * Pan-Sweden distribution centers – local presence.  
     * Budget-friendly – typically \~50% of new price or less  
     * [rekomo.se](https://www.rekomo.se/?srsltid=AfmBOoqU2ohc2dmqC6KgIBjGOZ1HrsNQYeJkBlUgsBpSFgQwbDEEUow7#:~:text=Vi%20k%C3%B6per%2C%20s%C3%A4ljer%20och%20hyr,Och%20f%C3%B6r%20din%20budget)  
     * .  
     * Rental options – helpful if a hotel wants short-term furniture (pop-up, or model room trials).  
     * Known brands and reliable quality (they stock top brands, ensuring base quality).  
   * Weaknesses:  
     * Does not cover all categories (no textiles, few beds).  
     * Focus on volume over bespoke – aesthetic variety might be limited; not ideal for luxury styling.  
     * Compliance support not as comprehensive (they will sell “as is” with basic checks, but client might have to handle special certifications).  
   * Best Fit: Budget hotels, hostels, or mid-scale chains focusing on cost savings; quick refurnishing needs (like a motel needing furniture in weeks). Projects where style can be more generic or client can accept slight mismatches to save cost.  
   * Price: Value/Mid – many items 50% of new, with deeper discounts on bulk. Transparent pricing online.  
   * Engagement: Contact their B2B Sales – possibly Magnus Änghede (they often have sales reps per region). They respond quickly to requests for quotes via website too. Could mention interest in large volume purchase to get a dedicated rep.  
   * Scenario Ratings:  
     * S1 Boutique: ⭐⭐ (2/5) – Not ideal for a design-focused boutique beyond supplying basics cheaply.  
     * S2 Chain: ⭐⭐⭐ (3/5) – Good for many items (chairs, desks) but lacking in guestroom-specific items like bed frames; would need supplement.  
     * S3 Luxury: ⭐ (1/5) – Not the right profile for high-end eclectic design.  
   * Compliance: Fire: 🟡 can be arranged (they might provide furniture that originally had certs, but they don’t treat or test themselves routinely). BVD: 🔴 gap (not their normal deliverable, though they might provide simple material info if asked). Testing: 🔴 none in-house (they rely on original build quality and their return policy).  
   * Contact: Customer service (enterprise) via \[email protected\] or phone 010-33 33 111  
   * [rekomo.se](https://www.rekomo.se/?srsltid=AfmBOoqU2ohc2dmqC6KgIBjGOZ1HrsNQYeJkBlUgsBpSFgQwbDEEUow7#:~:text=f%C3%B6r%20din%20budget)  
   * . Specific person: Anders Davidsson, Key Account (if exists). Warm intro via a known client who used them (maybe ask if any partner design firms have).  
   * First Contact: Focus on capabilities: “Can you source 100 identical \[item\]? Do you have storage if we want to collect items over time?” Also discuss refurbishment: they don’t reupholster normally, but ask if they partner for that if a client needs a different color etc. Clarify their warranty/return terms for used items in B2B.  
   * Engagement Tip: Perhaps use Rekomo in conjunction with others: sign a frame agreement for general used furniture supply at discount (for odds and ends), while using Tier1 for more specialized pieces.

TIER 2: Solid Commercial Operators with Hospitality Potential (Next 5\)

These have strong capabilities in B2B reuse and some relevant experience, but haven’t specialized in hospitality. With guidance and possibly partnership, they can contribute significantly to projects.

4. Recycling Partner (RP Kontorsmöbler) – *Turnkey office reuse solutions, emerging in other sectors.*  
   * Core: 20+ years refurbishing and furnishing offices with mix of new+used. Multi-location in Sweden (9 showrooms). Social enterprise roots.

   * Strengths:  
     * One-stop service: space planning, delivery, assembly provided.  
     * In-house refurbishment (furniture workshop and even ability to manufacture custom pieces from used materials).  
     * Environmental credentials (ISO 14001 likely, sustainability report published  
     * [rp.se](https://rp.se/#:~:text=,49)  
     * ).  
     * Flexible models: purchase, buy-back, even monthly rental of used furniture

     *  which could interest hotels wanting to avoid capex.  
     * Good nationwide coverage (sites from Malmö up to Uppsala).  
   * Weaknesses:  
     * Lesser known in hospitality means a learning curve on fire regs, etc.  
     * Possibly smaller volume capacity than Tier1 for very large identical orders (though they state can furnish whole offices).  
     * Style range mostly modern office; may need collaboration for more decorative pieces.  
   * Best Fit: Small hotels, offices turned boutique hotels, or any project wanting a mix of standard and quirky upcycled items. Good for common area furnishings, back-of-house office furniture for hotels, and potentially guestroom basics in mid-range properties.  
   * Estimated Price: Mid-market – Their model is cost-saving but includes service, so maybe 60% of new. They highlight it’s economically advantageous  
   * [rp.se](https://rp.se/#:~:text=Det%20finns%20egentligen%20tre%20sk%C3%A4l,grejer%20f%C3%A5r%20man%20ju%20en)  
   * .  
   * Scenario Contribution:

Documentation: 🟡 (they will provide basic data and any requested info, but not proactive unless asked). Testing: 🟡 (ad-hoc – they have not boasted of testing but would do if required, via partners).

     * ).  
     * Key People: CEO perhaps Andreas Göth (if from Allabolag).

     * ).  
     * Weakness bullet: Less hospitality track record; product line skewed to office functional designs; might need external lab for special compliance.

5. Kompanjonen (Dacke Consulting) – *Reuse consultants and brokers (“Återbruksmäklare”)*.  
   * Core: Helps organizations implement continuous reuse: inventory mapping, valuations, connecting sellers/buyers, managing deconstruction. Not a direct supplier but an orchestrator.  
   * Hospitality Potential: High as a consultant. They haven’t themselves delivered furniture to a hotel, but they have likely worked on project teams for sustainable building or interior projects (maybe helping a property company reuse elements in a renovation). They can greatly enhance a hospitality project by maximizing reuse of existing assets and sourcing unusual items.  
   * Strengths:  
     * Expert knowledge of reuse market and practices (10+ years specialty)  
     * [diva-portal.org](https://www.diva-portal.org/smash/get/diva2:1549635/FULLTEXT01.pdf#:~:text=3,att%20koppla%20ihop%20tillg%C3%A5ng%20och)  
     * .  
     * Broad network across Sweden (and even Nordic/European links via CCBuild etc.) to find stuff.  
     * Focus on process – ensuring from planning stage that reuse is integrated (this reduces risk of non-compliance or delays).  
     * Offers digital tools (Dacke.Online platform) that can inventory and share product data.  
     * Small but agile team – likely to go the extra mile to prove reuse concepts (passionate about mission).  
   * Weaknesses:  
     * Not a one-stop supplier – needs to work alongside material/furniture providers, which adds coordination.  
     * Scale limitations: only 5 employees means they consult, but for huge volumes, they rely on partners to physically handle goods.  
     * Their services add cost to a project (consulting fees), which some clients might skip thinking the supplier can do it all (but often supplier doesn’t do strategy – that’s where Kompanjonen adds value).  
   * Best Fit: Large complex projects with high reuse goals – e.g., a hotel undergoing sustainable certification, where a reuse expert on the team ensures targets are met. Also early-phase planning: if a client says “we have an old hotel we’re closing, and a new one we’re opening – how do we reuse content from old in new?”, Kompanjonen is perfect.  
   * Estimated Price: Consultancy rates (maybe in line with architecture/engineering fees). They might also take commission on any brokering (like a % of transaction value of goods). For budget, treat them as a professional service cost rather than direct procurement cost.  
   * Scenario Roles:  
     * S1: They would likely say timeline too short to involve them significantly, except possibly to source one missing piece.  
     * S2: Great role as project reuse manager – coordinate between demo of old interior and supply for new (could achieve high reuse ratio by planning).  
     * S3: As a curator finding unique pieces (like a specific statement furniture or antique doors).  
   * Tier rationale: They are Tier 2 because they strengthen the reuse outcome but cannot fulfill it alone.  
   * Engagement: Recommend engaging them early in project timeline – at concept stage – to identify opportunities for reuse (pre-demolition audits, etc.). Possibly integrate them into design team as specialist consultants (like an acoustician or lighting designer, but for reuse).  
   * Contact: Per Håkansson, CEO (he’s been vocal in media about reuse challenges  
   * [byggindustrin.se](https://www.byggindustrin.se/utveckling/hallbarhet/aterbruksexperter-om-marknadens-lycksokare-det-ar-rorigt-dar-ute-nu/#:~:text=%C3%85terbruksexperter%20om%20marknadens%20lycks%C3%B6kare%3A%20%E2%80%9DDet,i%20branschen%20kan%20leda)  
   * ). Email via dackeconsulting.com. A warm intro could be through SGBC (Green Building Council) or an architect who worked with them.  
   * Recommended Approach: Contract them for an “Återbruksinventering” (reuse audit) first – for example, if Fyra has an upcoming hotel reno, let Kompanjonen inventory existing furniture & materials to see what can be reused or sold. Then keep them on retainer to broker needed used items for the new design.  
   * One-page highlights:  
     * Core capability: Professional services – not providing products but enabling reuse flows (especially building materials, lighting, as well as furniture).  
     * Strengths: Lateral thinking, can handle unusual reuse requests, ensures documentation and due diligence.  
     * Weaknesses: Doesn’t physically deliver products; additional coordination needed.  
     * Best fit projects: Innovative sustainability-focused developments, clients aiming for zero-waste refurbishments.  
     * Scoring: Hard to score like a product supplier; think of them as enabler. They score high on adding value, but N/A on delivering items.  
   * Compliance: They help ensure compliance (🟢 for knowledge), they don’t hold certs themselves but will facilitate (like testing, 🟡 arranged).  
   * Client pitch benefit: “Kompanjonen will maximize what we can salvage and find for you, which could save X SEK and Y tonnes CO₂, and ensure we navigate any regs properly.” This appeals to forward-thinking hotel owners.  
6. Återbruksfabriken – *High-capacity building materials reuse hub (Sörmland).*  
   * Core: Collects and sells reused building materials with modern inventory system and 5000 m² warehouse  
   * [aterbruksfabriken.se](https://aterbruksfabriken.se/#:~:text=5000%20m%C2%B2%20lager%20och%20AI,teknik%20f%C3%B6rvandlar%20rester%20till%20resurser)  
   * . Essentially a B2B marketplace with physical stock for construction and interiors.  
   * Hospitality Application: Useful for interior build-outs – e.g., using reclaimed wood flooring, ceiling systems, doors, lighting infrastructure, even some fixtures in hotels. If a hotel project wants reused materials for credits or uniqueness (say, old bricks in a lobby wall or reclaimed timber beams), Återbruksfabriken can supply in a controlled way (with documentation). Possibly also fixtures like sinks or tile if going for a rustic sustainable vibe.  
   * Strengths:  
     * Large managed inventory ready to use, with technical details (they list items online, each with CO₂ savings  
     * [aterbruksfabriken.se](https://aterbruksfabriken.se/#:~:text=1%20st%20Rund%20metallkanal%2C%20gr%C3%A5%2C,kg%20CO2%20Se%20alla%20produkter)  
     * ).  
     * Focus on ease for builders: they pick-up from site and also deliver with needed paperwork, lowering effort for contractors to use reused parts.  
     * AI-driven catalog and presumably good matching of supply-demand.  
     * Partners like PreZero mean they have backing in waste management (scale-up potential).  
     * They explicitly aim for high volume reuse to meet future laws  
     * [aterbruksfabriken.se](https://aterbruksfabriken.se/#:~:text=P%C3%A5%20kort%20sikt%20fokuserar%20vi,att%20g%C3%A5%20direkt%20till%20deponi)  
     * , so they align with developers’ increasing needs.  
   * Weaknesses:  
     * Not furniture-focused (so can’t supply things like soft seating or casegoods).  
     * Still new – processes are evolving, could be some kinks in logistics.  
     * Location in Vrena – not near a big city; might need efficient transport planning for e.g., deliveries to Stockholm or Gothenburg (though Sörmland to Stockholm is fine).  
   * Best Fit: Projects with significant interior construction or refurbishment scope: e.g., converting an old building into a hotel where you need additional materials (and want them reused). Or new hotel construction aiming for circular economy points – they can procure reused materials like steel studs, glass partitions, etc. Also, if a hotel is demolishing an area (like an old wing), Återbruksfabriken could take those materials and maybe supply some needed in new build.  
   * Tier rationale: Tier 2 because for furnishings they’re not primary, but they complement by covering building elements which Tier 1 furniture suppliers don’t do.  
   * Engagement: Contact early in design if specific reused materials are desired so they can reserve or source them. Possibly coordinate via the general contractor normally, but Fyra can recommend them in specs (“contractor shall source materials from Återbruksfabriken or equivalent”).  
   * Contact: info@aterbruksfabriken.se, Tel 073-635 39 28  
   * [aterbruksfabriken.se](https://aterbruksfabriken.se/#:~:text=Kontakt)  
   * . Contact person might be Oscar Englund (if found via news).

     * Logo & Info: Modern logo (they brand themselves as a hub), address in Vrena, Sörmland.  
     * Key People: CEO likely from recycling industry.  
     * Strengths: 5000m² stock, AI inventory, offers both selling and buying services, documentation for green building, addresses contractor needs (including transport and storage).  
     * Weaknesses: Focused on materials, not a furniture outfitter; must integrate with construction schedule.  
     * Best for: salvage of excess materials, sustainable procurement for refurb (especially for achieving certifications or avoiding supply chain delays on new materials).  
   * Scenario roles:  
     * S1: Not much role (maybe if need some reclaimed wood for a feature wall).  
     * S2: Yes, if the reno needs construction materials (they might supply reused drywall, ventilation ducts to replace ones removed, etc. – they have ventilation listed  
     * [aterbruksfabriken.se](https://aterbruksfabriken.se/#:~:text=1%20st%20Gallerdon%20200mm%20Ok%C3%A4nd,kg%20CO2%20Se%20alla%20produkter)  
     * ).  
     * S3: Possibly provide unique reclaimed materials for décor (like old barnwood for a rustic luxury touch).  
   * Compliance: They ensure structural integrity etc. They mention meeting future building code, implying they do quality checks. Firewise, any building product they sell needs classification – e.g., reused doors might need to be fire doors or they’ll specify if not. They provide material passports if available. So: Fire: 🟡 (can supply rated items or info, but if not rated originally they won't upgrade them). Documentation: 🟢 (their selling point). Testing: 🟡 (likely check or test structural items).  
   * Engagement tip: If Fyra has a design that includes reclaimed materials, loop in Återbruksfabriken to see availability and get samples early, to ensure design compatibility and no surprises later.
