8. Place2Place.se – *Online marketplace for used furniture (B2C/B2B hybrid).*  
   * Core: E-commerce platform listing curated used furniture for home & office, with multiple items often available. Not hospitality-specific but can supply items that fit hospitality needs if available.  
   * Hospitality Potential: More for unique pieces or supplementing main procurement. E.g., find specific designer items, artwork, lighting, or fill small shortfalls (like needing 5 more chairs to match a set). Also useful for independent small hotel owners who want to pick interesting furniture piece by piece.  
   * Strengths:  
     * Modern, user-friendly shopping experience – easy to browse and order.  
     * Frequently updated stock, and they highlight quality (“kvalitetsmöbler”).  
     * Can handle secure payments and arrange shipping, which lowers risk dealing with individual sellers.  
     * In some cases, large lots are sold (like 68 chairs etc.), which could be treasure finds for the right project.  
   * Weaknesses:  
     * No project management or customization – items sold as listed.  
     * Availability is hit or miss – it’s driven by what is on the market. Not reliable for planning large uniform needs, only opportunistic buys.  
     * Not much in way of compliance support or warranties beyond consumer protections.  
   * Best Fit: Interior designers sourcing accent pieces or when you need to furnish a small area stylishly on budget. Or when you want to compare cost of a reused designer item vs new quickly by browsing. Could be part of a multi-source strategy: core items from a main supplier, and a few special pieces snapped up from Place2Place.  
   * Engagement: Simply use their online platform. Possibly talk to their team if doing a bulk purchase – they might offer a trade discount or help source via their contacts if you need something specific (maybe they can act as broker, since they likely have sellers in network).  
   * One-page summary:  
     * Contact: website place2place.se (no single contact person, it’s an online service).  
     * Strengths: broad selection including design brands, quick purchase process, can fulfill small orders fast.  
     * Weaknesses: not tailored to large projects, no guarantee of finding what you need.  
     * Best for: filling gaps, getting individual statement furniture.  
     * Tier rationale: Tier 2 because they add value to a project as a supplemental source rather than lead supplier.  
   * Scenario roles:  
     * S1: They could have provided maybe the mid-century style lounge chairs on short notice if listed.  
     * S2: Not ideal except maybe buying artwork or decorative lighting in bulk if listed.  
     * S3: Useful for finding some of those unique designer chairs or vintage pieces one by one.  
   * Compliance: Fire etc.: 🔴 none provided (buyer must handle).  
   * Overall engagement: Use them opportunistically; no need for a formal contract, just factor in some time for the design team to check listings and purchase items.  
9. DPJ Workspace (Återbruk department) – *Large office retailer with a used furniture segment.*  
   * Core: Sells primarily new office furniture, but has a curated used inventory online and in Stockholm showroom.  
   * Hospitality Potential: Minor. They could supply some used ergonomic chairs or conference furniture if needed cheaply. Possibly good source for demo-model pieces (like they had unused items with damaged packaging – essentially new but sold as used  
   * [dpj.se](https://www.dpj.se/504-aterbruk?srsltid=AfmBOopxmu9ulY1f9LhkLVWFzxnA1JXmbgBW3f1b91wT7lCt6hhkqlsz#:~:text=match%20at%20L187%20och%20k%C3%B6ps,produkt%20som%20annars%20skulle%20sl%C3%A4ngas)  
   * ). For hotels, maybe not a go-to, but an additional channel to find deals on some items.  
   * Strengths:  
     * Reliable company with logistics and customer service set up for businesses.  
     * Quick delivery of in-stock items (24h shipping for many items in Återbruk section).  
     * They classify condition clearly  
     * [dpj.se](https://www.dpj.se/504-aterbruk?srsltid=AfmBOopxmu9ulY1f9LhkLVWFzxnA1JXmbgBW3f1b91wT7lCt6hhkqlsz#:~:text=S%C3%A5%20h%C3%A4r%20klassificerar%20vi%20v%C3%A5ra,m%C3%B6bler%20f%C3%B6r%20%C3%A5terbruk)  
     * , so you know what you get.  
     * If buying both new and used from them, one invoice and integrated delivery, which simplifies things.  
   * Weaknesses:  
     * Very limited range in used section compared to specialized reuse operators.  
     * Focus on office styles and pieces – might not align with hospitality aesthetics.  
     * Policy “no returns on used items”  
     * [dpj.se](https://www.dpj.se/504-aterbruk?srsltid=AfmBOopxmu9ulY1f9LhkLVWFzxnA1JXmbgBW3f1b91wT7lCt6hhkqlsz#:~:text=Vid%20k%C3%B6p%20av%20%C3%A5terbruksvara%20%E2%80%93,Produkten%20%C3%A4r%20funktionellt)  
     *  – need to be sure before buying.  
   * Best Fit: If a project is already using DPJ for new items, check their used selection for any suitable piece to increase reuse quotient. Or if a hotel needs a few office setups (for back-office or in-room desks) on budget, DPJ used could supply those easily matching with new where needed.  
   * Engagement: Through their website or account manager if you have a DPJ contact. DPJ might not proactively push used items unless asked, so one has to inquire. They might have some internal list of “outlet items” not on website too.  
   * One-page bullet:  
     * Strengths: integration with new supply, condition grading, fast shipping, reputable retailer.  
     * Weaknesses: small fraction of their business, so offering is limited; no specialized services for reuse (no refurb, etc.).  
   * Scenario usage:  
     * S1: Maybe if the boutique wanted some design chairs cheap, DPJ might have an ex-demo designer chair at good price.  
     * S2: They could supply e.g., 120 used office chairs for back-of-house offices or meeting rooms in the hotel, but not really guest-facing stuff.  
     * S3: Unlikely to contribute meaningfully.  
   * Tier justification: At the lower end of Tier 2, included because they indicate mainstream acceptance of reuse and could be an easier internal sell for clients already working with them (less onboarding new vendors).  
   * Contact: DPJ sales (they have B2B sales line). Possibly mention interest in “Återbruk B2B portfolio” – they might assign a specialist who handles that part.

TIER 3: Niche/Emerging Players, High Potential (Last 3\)

These are smaller or specialized actors that might not have full capacity yet but offer unique capabilities that could be valuable in certain contexts or pilot projects.

10. Brattöns Återbruk AB – *Veteran salvage yard for building materials (Gothenburg).*  
    * Core: Selling salvaged building materials since 2012  
    * [blocket.se](https://www.blocket.se/butik/brattons-aterbruk#:~:text=Bratt%C3%B6ns%20%C3%85terbruk%20AB%20startades%20i,oss%20g%C3%A4rna%20p%C3%A5%20v%C3%A5ran)  
    * . Small team, very experienced in hands-on reuse.  
    * Potential: If a project in West Sweden wants authentic reused building elements (exposed brick, antique doors, vintage radiators, etc.), Brattöns is a treasure trove. They also might salvage items from older hotels or theaters that have historic character.  
    * Strengths:  
      * 10+ years of stock; they likely know every demolition in region to pick good stuff.  
      * Could provide elements that add a unique story (e.g., “these mirrors came from a 19th century hotel, now reinstalled here”).  
      * Flexibility and low cost – being small, they can negotiate trades, etc., and their prices are low (just need to cover overhead)  
      * [allabolag.se](https://www.allabolag.se/foretag/bratt%C3%B6ns-%C3%A5terbruk-ab/hisings-backa/tr%C3%A4-och-byggvaror-grossist/2K3N7JCI63IL2#:~:text=Oms%C3%A4ttning%202024%201%C2%A0902%20Tkr)  
      * .  
    * Weaknesses:  
      * One employee (per Allabolag) – limited scale for big fulfillments.  
      * Not geared for formal contracting or large deliveries; likely expects customers to arrange pickup.  
      * Inventory not cataloged extensively online – one may need to physically go and see or call to ask.  
    * Best Use: Specific architectural salvage needs in Gothenburg projects. Also as an educational example/pilot: working with them could support local circular economy and provide publicity (“heritage material reused in modern hotel”).  
    * Engagement: You’d basically call them or visit their warehouse. Possibly integrate them via the contractor (construction contractor in Gothenburg likely knows them, could source through them).  
    * One-page snippet:  
      * Org nr: 556900-9896 (for reference)  
      * [hitta.se](https://www.hitta.se/bratt%C3%B6ns+%C3%A5terbruk+ab/hisings+backa/iosbicnng#:~:text=Hitta%20www,har%20kunder%20%C3%B6ver%20hela%20Sverige)  
      * .  
      * Strengths: authentic materials, very sustainable (truly keeping things from landfill), personal expertise.  
      * Weakness: low capacity, minimal documentation (don’t expect BIM files or EPDs).  
    * Project scenario:  
      * S1: maybe supply some retro doors or panels for a thematic boutique environment.  
      * S2: if that chain hotel wanted to incorporate some reclaimed decor (like old windows as wall art), they could source from Brattöns.  
      * S3: absolutely could find statement vintage pieces (like a grand old wooden staircase piece to incorporate, etc.).  
    * Contact: Andreas Landqvist (founder) by phone 070-3509419  
    * [allabolag.se](https://www.allabolag.se/foretag/bratt%C3%B6ns-%C3%A5terbruk-ab/hisings-backa/tr%C3%A4-och-byggvaror-grossist/2K3N7JCI63IL2#:~:text=Telefon%20070)  
    * .  
    * Recommended for: pilot collaboration on something like creating a “reclaimed materials suite” in a hotel for marketing.  
    * Tier rationale: High-potential for adding heritage vibe, but not a volume workhorse.  
11. Malmö Återbyggdepå – *Municipal building material reuse depot (Malmö).*  
    * Core: City-run facility taking in donated building materials and reselling at low cost. Jointly run by Sysav (waste co.) and Malmö City  
    * [svenskbyggtidning.se](https://www.svenskbyggtidning.se/2024/05/14/ny-webbshop-framjar-cirkularitet-inom-byggsektorn/#:~:text=Ny%20webbshop%20fr%C3%A4mjar%20cirkularitet%20inom,Syftet%20%C3%A4r%20att)  
    * .  
    * Potential: For any project in Skåne aiming for circularity, it’s a local resource. Possibly useful for public areas of a hotel or for small renovations where one can find matching materials cheaply (like replacing broken tiles with ones from depot).  
    * Strengths:  
      * Longevity and community integration (20 years means they have a process).  
      * Diverse stock, sometimes surprising finds including large batches  
      * [malmo.se](https://malmo.se/Aktuellt/Artiklar-Malmo-stad/2024-05-15-Malmo-Aterbyggdepa-oppnar-webbshop.html#:~:text=Malm%C3%B6%20%C3%85terbyggdep%C3%A5%20%C3%B6ppnar%20webbshop%20Malm%C3%B6,till%20material%20i%20stora%20serier)  
      * .  
      * Very low prices (sometimes even free for certain items if donated).  
      * Good for PR/community goodwill: using the city’s reuse service shows local engagement.  
    * Weaknesses:  
      * Stock is hit-or-miss and not guaranteed for specific demands.  
      * They cater more to individuals; might not have formal processes for large B2B orders or reserved hold of items.  
      * Possibly limited hours and require pickup.  
    * Engagement: Have contractor or procurement person regularly check their inventory. Possibly attend when they opened the \*\*webshop (which they did, presumably allowing remote viewing)  
    * [malmo.se](https://malmo.se/Aktuellt/Artiklar-Malmo-stad/2024-05-15-Malmo-Aterbyggdepa-oppnar-webbshop.html#:~:text=Malm%C3%B6%20%C3%85terbyggdep%C3%A5%20%C3%B6ppnar%20webbshop%20Malm%C3%B6,till%20material%20i%20stora%20serier)  
    * .  
    * Use case: For example, if a boutique hotel in Malmö wants to incorporate some vintage radiators or extra parquet to match existing, this is a first stop. Or to source decorative elements like old columns, etc.  
    * Contact: Through Sysav or Malmö Serviceförvaltning (city’s service dept.). This is more of a resource than a partner.  
    * Tier rationale: Not a reliable supplier for a whole project, but a nice augmentation for sustainability goals or if budget-constrained in finishes.  
    * We include it as representative of such municipal depots in case the project context allows their use (especially for public-sector clients that prefer working with such).  
    * Compliance: They won't provide much beyond “sold as is”, but items like doors may not have fire labels etc. So careful what it’s used for.  
    * Overall, a Tier 3 for specific local sustainability integration.  
12. VarUnik (Circular Hospitality Service) – *New entry focusing on hotel circular solutions.*  
    * Core: As per news, VarUnik launched a circular subscription service for hotels (likely providing interior furnishings as a service focusing on sustainable materials)  
    * [ca.se](https://ca.se/category/b2b/#:~:text=Ny%20cirkul%C3%A4r%20abonnemangstj%C3%A4nst%20f%C3%B6r%20hotell)  
    * . This sounds like an innovative business specifically aimed at hotels.  
    * Status: It's an emerging concept (as of 2022\) and might still be ramping up. Possibly they partner with furniture makers or reuse suppliers to offer leasing of furniture and textiles to hotels, rotating them to refresh decor sustainably.  
    * Strengths:  
      * Hospitality-specific approach: they presumably understand hotel operations (they mention “hotels offered...” in that snippet  
      * [ca.se](https://ca.se/category/b2b/#:~:text=Ny%20cirkul%C3%A4r%20abonnemangstj%C3%A4nst%20f%C3%B6r%20hotell)  
      * ).  
      * Could handle a lot for the client: design, install, maintenance, replacement.  
      * Subscription model aligns with circular goals (furniture stays in loop).  
    * Weaknesses:  
      * Unproven track record, might be a startup with limited case studies.  
      * If focusing on new circular products more than reuse, it might not align 100% with “reuse existing pieces” – need to clarify their model.  
      * Possibly higher cost since it’s a premium new service.  
    * Recommended for: Forward-thinking hotel clients open to leasing furnishings or wanting to trial circular concepts like frequently changing interiors via reused pieces. Could be interesting for smaller boutique chains or eco-resorts.  
    * Engagement: Reach out to VarUnik (likely through their website or Cirkulära Affärer article’s contacts). See what exactly they offer – maybe they have packages (e.g., “circular hotel room package with furniture and linens, monthly fee”). If their offering fits, Fyra could collaborate with them rather than reinvent that wheel.  
    * Tier 3 because it’s early-stage and unknown reliability, but could be “Tier 1 of the future” if successful.  
    * Contact: Not publicly known, try the CA.se article contact or search VarUnik’s own site.  
    * Use in scenarios: Possibly an alternative approach to scenario 2 (Chain renovation) – instead of buying reused furniture, lease it from VarUnik’s circular program. They handle compliance and refresh, etc. This is speculative but worth exploring.

Shortlist Summary:

* Tier 1 (immediate go-to): YLLW Factory, Input/Greenified, (and possibly Rekomo for cost-focused cases). These we engage first for major projects where we need assurance and breadth.  
* Tier 2 (support & category specialists): RP, Kompanjonen, Återbruksfabriken, Spirec, Place2Place, DPJ Used – engaged as needed per project: e.g., Kompanjonen always as consultant in ambitious reuse project; Återbruksfabriken if building materials are in scope; Place2Place for design pieces.  
* Tier 3 (niche and pilots): Brattöns, Malmö Depå, VarUnik – consider for specific regional or innovative pilot projects to add value.

Each shortlisted operator brings unique value. The recommended approach is to build relationships with Tier 1 and key Tier 2 operators via smaller initial collaborations or meetings, then ramp up to formal framework agreements for future projects.

### **Contact Strategy & Introduction Tips:**

For each Tier 1-2 operator, approach with tailored angle:

* YLLW: Emphasize our commitment to high-quality design and sustainability; request to visit their refurb factory to see quality firsthand – builds relationship.  
* Input: Possibly engage via an existing relationship if any (maybe Fyra or the client has worked with Input on new FF\&E; bring up interest in Greenified now).  
* Rekomo: Reach out for a specific price quote to gauge their responsiveness and pricing – they like concrete inquiries.  
* RP: Suggest a trial project (like furnishing a small common area with their furniture) and ask for references from previous clients to assure stakeholders.  
* Kompanjonen: Invite them to do a short workshop with our design team about “reuse possibilities in hospitality” – free knowledge sharing that could lead to hiring them.  
* Återbruksfabriken: If a project is near Sörmland, plan a half-day charrette where their team meets the architects/contractors to discuss what materials could be reused – showing proactive collaboration.  
* Spirec: Might invite them to present at a sustainability forum or include them in an innovation section of a project (especially if client is interested in supporting startups).  
* Place2Place: No formal contact needed beyond using site, but could speak with them about maybe setting up alerts for certain items we frequently need (if they have that feature or a concierge service).  
* DPJ: Talk to our DPJ sales rep to ensure they list any suitable outlet items when quoting new stuff – i.e., integrate it into procurement process.  
* VarUnik: If their offering is live, schedule a meeting to learn details; possibly position it to a progressive client as an option (and get VarUnik to present to them).

This shortlist ensures Fyra has a range of partners to cover all bases of reused supply. Tier 1s can be immediately relied on for primary needs, Tier 2s can fill gaps and add expertise, and Tier 3s can be experimented with or brought in for specialized value. Moving forward, establishing framework agreements or preferred partnerships with Tier 1 and key Tier 2 operators (like YLLW, Input, RP, Kompanjonen) will smooth future project workflows and possibly secure better pricing and priority service. Tier 3 can be engaged on a project-by-project basis or through informal networks.

## **G. Operational Guidance for Fyra (Implementation Playbook)**

Beyond identifying suppliers, success in integrating reuse into hospitality projects depends on strategy and process. This section provides actionable guidance on how Fyra can effectively work with multiple reuse vendors, manage sourcing through project phases, mitigate risks, control costs, and communicate the value to clients.

### **Multi-Vendor Strategy**

It’s likely no single vendor will cover everything for a complex project. Therefore, Fyra should plan a multi-vendor collaboration model:

* Segment by Category: Determine which supplier leads for each category. For instance:  
  * Furniture & casegoods – lead by YLLW or Input (Tier 1).  
  * Textiles (if any reused) – maybe VarUnik or new if reuse isn't feasible.  
  * Lighting – possibly sourced via Kompanjonen’s network or purchased new if no safe reused available.  
  * Building materials – via Återbruksfabriken or salvage yards.  
* Combine Strengths: Example for a boutique hotel: YLLW for bulk furniture, Kompanjonen to source unique decor \+ coordinate salvage, RP to provide some custom upcycled feature pieces, and a local carpenter to integrate reclaimed materials from Återbruksfabriken into built-in elements. Each has defined scope.  
* Define Roles & Boundaries: Use clear scopes in contracts or purchase orders: who supplies what, who is responsible for refurbishing vs just supplying as-is, etc. Avoid overlaps (two suppliers providing same item type) unless as fallback.  
* Lead Coordinator: Decide whether Fyra (as designer) will coordinate these vendors or if one vendor can act as a principal contractor. Often, on furniture, either the client’s procurement or Fyra might have to take on the coordination if multiple contracts. If a general contractor is willing, they could incorporate reuse suppliers under their umbrella (especially for building materials). However, many GC’s are unfamiliar with managing these parties, so Fyra may need to step up in coordination.  
* Regular Joint Check-ins: Host periodic coordination meetings (virtually if needed) with all suppliers’ reps involved in the project. Ensure the timeline is understood (e.g., “All reused items must be on site by X date for installation”) and dependencies (e.g., building materials delivered before furniture install).  
* Logistics Integration: Align delivery schedules and responsibilities: If multiple suppliers deliver around the same time, possibly consolidate shipments to site if feasible (to reduce chaos and site handling). Maybe hire one 3PL logistics firm to collect from various suppliers into one delivery – though this adds cost, it might simplify site management.  
* Quality Control: With multi-suppliers, plan for an on-site (or at warehouse) inspection when items arrive from each. If an item from vendor A doesn’t meet expectation, better to catch it before it’s needed on site so vendor B or backup plan can fill in.  
* Framework Agreements Across Vendors: If Fyra will frequently collaborate with, say, YLLW and Kompanjonen together, consider an MOU between those vendors or at least share with them that they’ll often work on same projects. They might then establish a relationship too (for instance YLLW might rely on Kompanjonen as a subcontractor for sourcing rare pieces). Encouraging such partnerships can smooth multi-vendor integration because they become familiar with each other’s working style.

Potential Constellations:

* For a standard project, likely Furniture Hub (Type A) \+ Consultant/Broker (Type B) \+ possibly Material hub (Type A for materials) forms a robust trio. Example: YLLW \+ Kompanjonen \+ Återbruksfabriken.  
* If one domain is weak with one vendor, complement with a specialist: e.g., YLLW doesn't do lighting, so engage a lighting refurb specialist or buy efficient new lighting (with replaceable parts for future circularity).  
* For textiles, since reuse is tricky, maybe plan them new but sustainable (or try VarUnik’s approach).  
* Central Document: Keep a matrix of who supplies what item for tracking.

Coordinating 2-3 suppliers effectively:

* Set up a shared schedule (all suppliers input their lead times and key dates).  
* Use a collaboration tool or simply email threads that include all relevant parties to avoid siloed info.  
* Nominate one point of contact at Fyra (or client side) to interface with all, to avoid confusion.  
* Ensure each supplier knows about the others where relevant (like the furniture supplier should know what salvage doors are coming so they can coordinate finishes perhaps).  
* Address conflicts upfront: e.g., if reused items end up delayed, which supplier or plan covers the gap? Bake contingency into timeline (deliver a bit earlier, have spares lined up).  
* Rammeavtale (Framework agreements): If possible, sign a framework with top suppliers for X volume over Y years at agreed discount, which can cover multiple projects. It gives them incentive (committed pipeline) and gives Fyra simplicity (no need to renegotiate each time). E.g., a frame agreement with YLLW for up to 5M SEK of furniture supply per year for various projects with pre-negotiated pricing terms. Another with Kompanjonen for consulting services on call at a fixed day rate. This streamlines future multi-supplier management and can encourage them to allocate resources for Fyra’s needs preferentially.

### **Sourcing Process Framework**

Implement a structured sourcing process aligned with typical project phases: Phase 1: Pre-Design (6–8 months before construction start)

Goal: Identify reuse opportunities early.

* Early Vendor Engagement: Inform selected reuse suppliers of an upcoming project as soon as basic program known. E.g., tell YLLW “we might need \~100 chairs, 50 beds in about 8 months for X hotel.” This lets them keep an eye out for relevant inventory (maybe they’ll hold a batch they come across).  
* Reuse Feasibility Study: Work with a consultant (Kompanjonen) to do a *reuse audit* of any existing assets. If it’s a renovation, inventory existing furniture: what can be retained or refurbished? If new build, outline which elements could feasibly be reused (maybe materials for interior, some furnishings from elsewhere).  
* Long-List Available Inventory: Reach out to major suppliers to get a preliminary sense of what they currently have or can source. E.g., ask Rekomo or YLLW for current stock lists of chairs or tables that might suit the project’s style. CCBuild Marketplace search as well for key items (like “doors B-s1,d0”).  
* Prelim Budget & Sustainability Impact: Work up an initial scenario: “If we reuse X, Y, Z items, we anticipate \~??% cost saving and ?? tons CO₂ saved.” Use supplier input (some provide these calculations  
* [yllw.com](https://www.yllw.com/yllw-factory#:~:text=%2B)  
* ).  
* Client Buy-in: Present this to client early to get conceptual approval for pursuing reuse. Showing data helps overcome skepticism.

Phase 2: Design Development (4–6 months before construction)

Goal: Confirm specific reused items and integrate into design.

* Confirm Availability: Re-contact suppliers to verify which items from the long-list are still available and suitable. Reserve key pieces now if possible (some suppliers may hold items with a deposit or agreement).  
* Finalize Specifications with Reuse in Mind: For each item category in the design (chairs, lamps, flooring), decide if it will be reused or new. If reused, specify something like “Existing or Reused \[item\] as per supplier X, model/description ...”. Allow some flexibility (“or equivalent”) in spec to adjust depending on final find.  
* Integrate into Drawings/Models: Incorporate reused item dimensions and visuals into design documents. E.g., get product images from supplier for client approval. If items are mismatched set (like eclectic chairs), prepare a *lookbook* for client showing the chosen pieces.  
* Begin Testing/Certification: For any reused item that needs certification (like fabric flammability or load testing), initiate that now. Send a sample to lab, or have supplier do it. This gives buffer for any failures or re-treatments.  
* Reserve Items: Ideally, have suppliers mark items as “sold” or reserved by the end of DD. Possibly sign conditional purchase orders contingent on final quantity needs. Some might want a down payment to hold items (especially if storing them for months). Build that into budget.  
* Identify Gaps: If something you hoped to reuse isn’t available or client rejected a reused option, flag it and pivot to sourcing new or alternate reuse source.

Phase 3: Procurement (2–4 months before installation)

Goal: Formalize orders and prep logistics.

* Place Orders / Sign Contracts: With final quantities and specs, issue purchase orders to each reuse supplier. Ensure all terms cover conditions like delivery date, installation responsibility, warranty terms, and what happens if an item is found unacceptable on arrival (return/exchange policy).  
* Quality Inspection Prior to Delivery: If possible, schedule a visit to supplier’s site to inspect a batch (especially if large volume). E.g., check one of the refurbished chairs from YLLW to approve the upholstery job, etc. This can be done by Fyra or client’s rep. Many suppliers will send detailed photos if an in-person isn't feasible.  
* Finalize Logistikk Plan: Work out exact delivery windows with each supplier. Coordinate with general contractor or site manager about receiving reused items (they might need storage on site or immediate placement to avoid clutter). If items need assembly or installation, confirm who does that (some furniture suppliers do assembly, some not).  
* Packaging & Transport Arrangements: Encourage sustainable packaging – many reuse suppliers already minimize packaging (since items aren’t in original boxes). Ensure they still protect items adequately. Possibly arrange consolidated transport: e.g., if Kompanjonen sourced some items from various places, maybe they bring all to one warehouse (like YLLW’s) and then one truck to site – reducing handling.  
* Update Documentation: Gather final compliance docs from suppliers now to include in project handover. E.g., fire certificates after treatment, warranty statements. This avoids chasing papers after install.

Phase 4: Delivery & Installation

Goal: Smooth on-site execution of reused items installation.

* Coordinated Delivery: Stagger deliveries to avoid chaos. For example, building materials from Återbruksfabriken might come during rough construction, furniture from YLLW comes during FF\&E stage later. Provide suppliers with a schedule and site contact person.  
* Site Handling: Check items off against the inventory list as they arrive. Any damage or missing items – notify supplier immediately (so they can possibly replace or repair).  
* Assembly & Placement: If suppliers provide install, supervise it to ensure correct placement per design. If not, arrange a skilled crew (maybe same as who installs new furniture). Reused items sometimes need a wipe-down after transport, or small adjustments (tightening screws that loosened, etc.).  
* Final Inspection: Once installed, do a walkthrough with client and ideally the supplier’s rep for major items. Ensure client is satisfied that reused items look good (this helps cement buy-in – seeing them in place looking “like new” or with desired character).  
* Punch List: Document any issues: e.g., a drawer that sticks, a lamp that doesn’t work. Because items are used, there might be a slightly higher punch list than new, but reputable suppliers check functionality thoroughly. Give the supplier a chance to fix these promptly (some may have sent a couple extra spares for such an event, especially if arranged).

Phase 5: Post-Occupancy

Goal: Maintain satisfaction and close the loop.

* Warranty & Support: Reiterate the warranty period and process to client. E.g., “YLLW provides 3-year warranty, if anything fails, we contact them and they will repair or replace.” Monitor any early issues – reused furniture should hold up, but if something does break in first months, it’s key to have supplier honor warranty to keep client confident.  
* Maintenance Plan: Provide any maintenance info from suppliers to the facility management: e.g., cleaning instructions for refurbished wood, or periodic tightening schedule for reused chairs (just as one would with new). If some pieces are antiques needing gentle care, note that.  
* Evaluate Performance: After a few months, get feedback: are there complaints from staff or guests about the reused items? Use that feedback to adjust future reuse specs (maybe next time choose more robust items or do additional refurb steps).  
* Take-Back or End-of-Life: If any contract or arrangement for end-of-life exists (some suppliers might have offered buy-back guarantee at end of use), note it in asset register. For example, YLLW might tag furniture with a QR code linking to a “product passport” so that in 10 years, it can be easily circulated again  
* [yllw.com](https://www.yllw.com/yllw-factory#:~:text=H%C3%A5llbarhet%20p%C3%A5%20riktigt)  
* . Ensure client is aware so they don’t just toss them later.  
* Document Outcomes: Very important – compile the results: % reused by value or quantity, CO₂ saved (suppliers like Återbruksfabriken and YLLW gave numbers  
* [yllw.com](https://www.yllw.com/yllw-factory#:~:text=65000)  
* , add them up), any cost savings realized versus budget, and qualitative successes (e.g., “guests love the story behind the lobby chandelier that was reused from an opera house”). This not only helps with immediate client satisfaction (they can use it in PR), but also provides Fyra with case studies to persuade future clients.

### **Risk Mitigation Strategies**

Using reused elements introduces some uncertainties. Proactively managing those risks ensures the project stays on track. Below are key risk areas and mitigation approaches: Availability Risk (Will we find enough of what we need?):

* Buffer alternatives: During design, identify a fallback new product “or equal” for critical items. In specs, literally write reused option and “or new equivalent if reuse not available.” So if by mid-project the reused supply falls through, the contractor can procure the new one (though at cost of lost sustainability goal, it's a safety net).  
* Spec flexibility: Use performance or aesthetic specs that allow multiple sources. E.g., specify “solid wood chairs of similar design, any of oak/beech/ash acceptable” rather than a very specific model – broadens chances of finding matches in reuse market.  
* Advance procurement: Secure reused items as early as possible (even storing them) to remove risk of last-minute unavailability. Yes, this ties up some capital/storage, but it's worth it for crucial items.  
* Hybrid strategy for critical items: For extremely critical components where failure to find reused could delay project (like bed frames), consider a plan to mix new and used. For example, procure 50% new beds and 50% reused, thereby reducing risk and still achieving some reuse. Or plan to buy new but include a contract clause that supplier will incorporate some reused parts (like manufacturer using reclaimed wood panels).  
* Supplier guarantees: If working with a major supplier (Input, YLLW), negotiate that if they cannot source enough reused units, they will supply new ones of similar style at a pre-agreed price. This way the project won’t stall; cost might rise slightly but at least continuity. (Some might agree to this since they often sell new too).

Quality Risk (Will items be good enough in look and function?):

* Inspection and sample approvals: Do not skip reviewing samples – whether physically or via detailed photos. For reused items, each piece can differ slightly (patina, etc.), so define acceptable range. Approve a benchmark item that others should match.  
* Mandatory pre-delivery inspection: Write into purchase orders that Fyra or client reserves right to inspect items at supplier’s warehouse and reject subpar ones before shipping. This pushes suppliers to self-qc strictly, knowing we’ll check.  
* Rejection terms: Ensure contracts allow rejecting or returning items that don’t meet agreed condition. E.g., “Any chairs with more than minor scratch on visible parts will be rejected and must be replaced or refunded.” So supplier might send a few extras anticipating that, or at least agree to remedy.  
* Refurbishment backup plan: If an item arrives not meeting expectation but is salvageable (maybe a deeper clean or an extra coat of finish would fix it), have a plan – either the supplier sends a person to touch up on site, or have a handyman on standby to do minor fixes (and charge back to supplier if responsibility). For large-scale, maybe keep an on-call furniture medic during install week.  
* Gradation approach: In design, if using reused items with some wear (like vintage pieces), place them in contexts that are forgiving. For example, slightly worn tables can be in a casual bar area with dim lighting, not in the bright lobby centerpiece. Use “Grade B” condition items where they won’t be scrutinized as heavily, save “Grade A” for focal points.  
* Client expectation management: Show the client images of the actual pieces ahead and get sign-off that they accept the character. This prevents later “I didn’t expect it to look so old” surprises. If the client is uneasy, err on the side of over-refurbishing (like full re-varnish) to reach like-new finish.

Compliance Risk (Will items meet fire codes, etc. in time?):

* Early testing: As noted, test representative samples of reused materials for fire performance at least 4+ months before install. For example, take one reused armchair, have it tested to EN 1021 now – if it fails, you know you must treat foam/fabric or choose a different product.  
* Get certificates for original items: For reused items from known manufacturers, contact manufacturer early for any test documentation (some keep records or can confirm materials used). This can save doing new tests if original spec suffices.  
* Alternate supplier backup: If crucial compliance (like all mattresses must be flame retardant by law) and reused ones can’t be confirmed or treated in time, have an alternate lined up. Eg: “If reused mattresses fail TB603, we will purchase new certified ones from supplier Z by week X.”  
* Regulatory consultant: For complex regulatory questions, have an expert (maybe a fire engineer) on call. Kompanjonen or Input’s specialists can advise or we might involve a consultant to liaise with authorities if using something unconventional (like reusing an old structural element in a building – get engineer sign-off).  
* Document everything: Keep an organized folder of compliance documents from suppliers, and review for completeness a month or two before opening. This way if anything is missing, you have time to chase. Many reuse projects falter in documentation in last minute; proactive tracking prevents that.

Logistics Risk (Lead time delays, damage in transit, site handling issues):

* Lead time buffers: As a rule, add \~25% buffer to reuse items’ lead time. Because unexpected things (like needing extra refurb or transport snag) happen. E.g., if supplier says “we need 8 weeks to refurbish,” plan for 10\. Start processes early (as outlined in sourcing timeline).  
* On-site storage plan: If reused items arrive before they can be installed (maybe to ensure they’re secured early), have a safe, dry storage area arranged. Could be a rented container or a room at site sealed off. Ensure insurance covers these stored items. Also plan security if they are valuable pieces.  
* Contingency storage: In phased projects, maybe store second phase furniture off-site until needed (some suppliers will hold until call-off – formalize that). Återbruksfabriken even offers storage as a service  
* [aterbruksfabriken.se](https://aterbruksfabriken.se/#:~:text=Tj%C3%A4nster)  
*  (for construction materials).  
* Backup transport arrangements: Sometimes smaller suppliers might have weaker logistics (Brattöns might rely on a third-party trucker who cancels). Maintain a list of trucking companies or man-with-a-van services in region as backup. If a supplier fails to ship on promised day, we can send our contracted truck to pick up. Build a little budget for emergency shipping.  
* Installation scheduling: Spread out installation of reused items rather than doing all last day. Because if an item is missing a part or wrong, you want a day or two to fix it. For example, install reused casegoods one week, and reused chairs the next – not all on same day as opening.  
* Communication: Maintain frequent contact with suppliers as delivery nears. Check in “are we on track for X date?” a couple of weeks out. It’s easier to adapt at T-2 weeks than T-2 days.  
* O\&M Manuals inclusion: Consider packaging reused items info in the operational manual for the hotel. This is not exactly a risk, but ensures facility staff treat items correctly (e.g., they know that the wood table has been waxed not lacquered, so they use proper cleaning – avoiding inadvertent damage which might be mistaken as quality issue).

Overall, mitigating risk for reuse largely means proactive planning and flexibility – earlier engagement, buffers, backup options. Many of these strategies mirror what one would do with any custom or non-standard items in a project (e.g., custom furniture often has similar risks). By institutionalizing these steps, Fyra can handle reuse with minimal surprises and build a reputation for smoothly delivering such projects.

### **Cost Management Framework**

To ensure cost-effectiveness and transparency with reused furnishings, adopt a structured approach to budgeting and cost control: Budget Model and Savings Calculation:

* Start with a baseline: what would the cost be if everything were procured new (maybe mid-range products)? Then as reuse options firm up, calculate expected cost for those. Present to client as e.g., “By sourcing X% reused, we expect Y% direct cost savings.” This sets expectation that reuse isn’t just environmentally good, but also giving financial benefit (often \~30-50% savings as indicated earlier).  
* E.g., “We plan 70% of furniture by value to be reused, which should yield \~45% cost reduction on those items, equating to \~15% of total FF\&E budget saved.” Provide range if uncertain, to be safe.  
* Also mention potential avoidance of disposal costs if reusing existing (like “we saved SEK 100k in disposal by repurposing your old furniture” – not directly in procurement budget but still a cost factor).  
* Ensure the budget has contingencies: It’s prudent to allocate, say, 10-15% of reused items’ budget as contingency (for unexpected refurb cost or replacements). This is similar to how new FF\&E might have 5-10% contingency but reuse can have a bit more.

Hidden Costs to Include:

* Testing/Certification costs: e.g., flame-proofing treatment cost per item, lab test fees – include these in budget line items. Either ask supplier to include in their quote or set aside a separate budget for compliance. Being explicit avoids these becoming surprises later.  
* Transport & Handling: Sometimes reused items require more handling (picking up from multiple locations, re-delivery after refurb). Ensure quotes include delivery; if not, budget separately. Possibly higher installation cost if items are assorted (installers may need more time to adjust pieces versus uniform new ones).  
* Refurbishment & Cleaning: If not already in supplier price. For instance, maybe you get chairs free from a donor but then pay an upholsterer – account for that.  
* Storage: If renting storage space or containers due to early sourcing, include that cost for the months needed.  
* Design Integration costs: The extra design time to coordinate and adapt reused items – should be accounted for in Fyra’s fees if significant. Educate the client that some of the cost saved on furniture might be spent in design/coordination to do reuse right. Usually, it’s still net savings, but good to highlight service costs.  
* Repairs & spares: Possibly plan small budget for spares (e.g., buying 5 extra chairs out of 100 in case some break or for future replacement). Many reuse deals smartly include a few surplus pieces if available – budget for it or negotiate it.  
* Contingency for new purchases: In worst case scenario if a reused batch falls through late and you must buy new overnight (worst-case), allocate some contingency. Or have it covered by savings margin.

Volume Discounts Negotiation:

* Even though items are used, you can negotiate volume pricing especially with dealers like Rekomo or YLLW. If you’re taking 100 pieces, ask for say 5-10% off their list price of used item. Many will agree because moving large volume at once is a win for them too.  
* If working with multiple categories from one supplier (like ordering chairs, tables, cabinets all from YLLW), negotiate a package price rather than item-by-item.  
* For suppliers that normally do auction or single sale (Place2Place), see if they can do a deal offline for bulk (maybe outside their normal process).  
* Frame agreements can include discount tiers: e.g., “if in a year we buy \> SEK 1M from you, apply an additional 5% rebate” – encourages them to track and reward loyalty.  
* Use competition politely: if two suppliers can supply something, get quotes from both to leverage best price (but weigh quality differences).  
* Note: hyper focus on lowest cost can undermine reuse quality (we don’t want cheapest junk). So balance cost negotiation with maintaining goodwill of suppliers who are giving extra service (like testing or customization). Aim for fair price that acknowledges their value-add, but ensure client sees net savings.

Total Cost of Ownership (TCO) vs Initial Cost:

* Articulate to client that reused items can have lower TCO as well:  
  * Often higher quality for the price (e.g., buying a 10-year-old top-brand chair for 1/3 price, it might last another 10 years easily – whereas a cheap new chair at same price might break in 5).  
  * Many reuse suppliers give warranties similar to new, so maintenance/replacement cost in initial years is covered  
  * [rp.se](https://rp.se/#:~:text=,49)  
  * .  
  * There’s also potential residual value: reused furniture might be re-reused or sold again, thus the hotel could recoup some value at end-of-life. For new furniture, resale value also exists but you’ve paid more upfront. Emphasize circular approach can include “take-back at project end” – some suppliers might even include that in contract (e.g., YLLW might buy back furniture at 10% of cost after 5 years). That further reduces effective cost.  
* Include intangible but meaningful factors: Reused items already off-gassed (so better indoor air faster), which may reduce any need for specialized air filtering after install or improve guest satisfaction early on (new furniture smell can bother some).  
* Possibly insurance: confirm if any differences (generally no, furniture is furniture).  
* Use a lifecycle period assumption: e.g., assume both new and reused furniture will serve 10 years; if reused is of good quality, it should. Show the cost per year of new vs reused (often dramatically favoring reused).  
* If leasing models are used (like VarUnik’s subscription), present it like an OpEx vs CapEx shift and mention avoidance of disposal cost at end – they handle it.

Budget Monitoring:

* Track expenditures specifically on reused procurement vs budget. Because reused deals can sometimes be more fluid (e.g., final count changes if they send spares or you drop some items). Keep a close eye and update cost plan whenever a change in strategy (like deciding to buy new for an item) occurs so client isn’t surprised.  
* Clarify early how any supplier cost overruns will be handled: e.g., if a refurb turns out more expensive, is that risk on supplier (fixed quote) or on client (time & materials)? Prefer fixed quotes, but some small shops might insist on T\&M since they can’t predict unknown repairs. If T\&M, build extra contingency for that.

### **Client Communication Toolkit**

To get clients (especially conservative ones) on board with reuse, and to keep them confident throughout, it’s vital to communicate effectively: Pitching Reuse to Conservative Hotel Clients:

* Lead with Success Stories: Share case studies of successful reuse in hospitality or analogous contexts. If none from hotels in Sweden, use international examples or office examples. E.g., “X Hotel in London furnished 50% of their rooms with restored mid-century furniture, resulting in a unique guest experience and 40% cost savings  
* [wihlborgs.se](https://www.wihlborgs.se/sv/om-oss/nyheter/hallbar-fastighetsutveckling/minskad-klimatpaverkan-med-aterbruk-av-kontorsmobler/#:~:text=,vd%20och%20grundare%20av%20Soeco)  
* .” Or Wihlborgs offices case, albeit not hotel, shows corporate acceptance  
* [wihlborgs.se](https://www.wihlborgs.se/sv/om-oss/nyheter/hallbar-fastighetsutveckling/minskad-klimatpaverkan-med-aterbruk-av-kontorsmobler/#:~:text=Ramavtalet%20som%20Wihlborgs%20har%20med,avkall%20p%C3%A5%20funktion%20eller%20design)  
* .  
* Align with Their Goals: If the client has stated sustainability goals or needs to improve ESG, highlight how reuse directly addresses that. If their concern is budget, focus on cost savings. If branding, focus on uniqueness and storytelling potential.  
* Visual Aids: Create mood boards or renderings that incorporate reused pieces so they can “see” it will still look high-quality. Often visual proof eases the fear that reused \= shabby. Before-and-after refurb photos can wow them (e.g., show a ragged chair turned like-new after YLLW works on it  
* [wihlborgs.se](https://www.wihlborgs.se/sv/om-oss/nyheter/hallbar-fastighetsutveckling/minskad-klimatpaverkan-med-aterbruk-av-kontorsmobler/#:~:text=dem%20klassiska%20designm%C3%B6bler%20i%20behov,l%C3%A4gre%20pris%20och%20l%C3%A4gre%20klimatp%C3%A5verkan)  
* ).  
* Quantify Environmental Impact: Provide a simple metric: “By reusing, we avoid emitting X tons CO₂.” Maybe translate that to equivalents (like "equal to flying around the world Y times" or "taking Z cars off road for a year") to make it relatable. Many execs respond to numbers especially if they have to report to stakeholders.  
* Emphasize Quality & Warranty: Make it clear we’re not putting in garage-sale junk; these are high-quality items often better built than cheaper new alternatives, and suppliers stand by them with warranties. So the risk to operations is low. Mention: “These items have been professionally refurbished and tested – essentially as good as new with a full warranty, so your guest experience or maintenance costs should not suffer.  
* [wihlborgs.se](https://www.wihlborgs.se/sv/om-oss/nyheter/hallbar-fastighetsutveckling/minskad-klimatpaverkan-med-aterbruk-av-kontorsmobler/#:~:text=,vd%20och%20grundare%20av%20Soeco)  
* [rp.se](https://rp.se/#:~:text=,49)  
* ”  
* Sustainability Branding: Position reuse as a marketing advantage: The hotel can promote itself as eco-friendly, with real actions not just greenwashing. Today’s travelers, especially in Nordics, appreciate sustainability. Possibly mention certification points (if pursuing Nordic Swan or LEED, reuse gives them credits).  
* Pilot / Model Room: If they’re still hesitant, suggest trying it in one model room or one area first. “Let’s furnish one sample room with reused pieces and see – you only commit to full rollout if you’re happy.” The low cost of reused might allow this pilot without significant budget risk.

References and Testimonials:

* Provide reference clients they can talk to. Perhaps a facility manager from an office that used Soeco, who can attest the furniture was great and service good  
* [wihlborgs.se](https://www.wihlborgs.se/sv/om-oss/nyheter/hallbar-fastighetsutveckling/minskad-klimatpaverkan-med-aterbruk-av-kontorsmobler/#:~:text=Att%20samverka%20med%20hyresg%C3%A4ster%20kring,Wihlborgs%20s%C3%A5%20kallade%20Gr%C3%B6na%20hyresavtal)  
* .  
* Or any small hotel that quietly bought used from an auction – if we can find one willing to share experience (maybe not many yet, but there might be a boutique B\&B somewhere doing it).  
* Use quotes: e.g., Wihlborgs’ sustainability head saying reuse is key  
* [wihlborgs.se](https://www.wihlborgs.se/sv/om-oss/nyheter/hallbar-fastighetsutveckling/minskad-klimatpaverkan-med-aterbruk-av-kontorsmobler/#:~:text=ImageStaffan%20Fredlund%20%C3%A4r%20milj%C3%B6,klimatchef%20p%C3%A5%20Wihlborgs)  
* , or Samhall’s comment that difference from new is negligible  
* [rp.se](https://rp.se/#:~:text=Claes%20Seldeby%20VD%20f%C3%B6r%20Ahlsell,Sverige%20AB)  
*  – those help build trust.

Handling Risk Concerns in Messaging (“We take the risk, you get the benefit”):

* Explain how we have structured plan to mitigate all risks (as we indeed do above). For each common worry:  
  * *“What if it looks bad or inconsistent?”* – We will carefully select and refurbish items to meet design standards; client gets to approve samples to ensure satisfaction.  
  * *“What if something breaks?”* – We have warranties and spares ready; no greater downtime risk than new.  
  * *“What if we can’t find enough?”* – We have multiple sources and backup options identified, plus conventional new product fallback if absolutely needed. We will not compromise the project timeline.  
  * Essentially, position Fyra (and partners) as absorbing extra effort: “We as the design team will handle the complexity behind the scenes, so you don’t feel any more burden than a normal project. You’ll simply see the end result and the reports of savings.”  
* Possibly propose a contractual arrangement: if reuse fails to deliver promised savings or timeline, some penalty or revert to plan B at no additional cost beyond agreed contingency. This assures them they won’t be left holding the bag if something goes wrong. (We have to be careful not to overexpose ourselves though; that’s why careful planning is key.)

Case Story Portfolio (Before/After images, finished projects):

* Put together a mini-portfolio PDF: show a piece of furniture “before” (worn condition) and “after” refurbishment in situ at a project. E.g., Soeco had images of chairs reupholstered looking like new  
* [wihlborgs.se](https://www.wihlborgs.se/sv/om-oss/nyheter/hallbar-fastighetsutveckling/minskad-klimatpaverkan-med-aterbruk-av-kontorsmobler/#:~:text=I%20Soecos%20lokaler%20st%C3%A5r%20m%C3%A4ngder,l%C3%A4gre%20pris%20och%20l%C3%A4gre%20klimatp%C3%A5verkan)  
* [wihlborgs.se](https://www.wihlborgs.se/sv/om-oss/nyheter/hallbar-fastighetsutveckling/minskad-klimatpaverkan-med-aterbruk-av-kontorsmobler/#:~:text=Allt%20fler%20f%C3%A5%20upp%20%C3%B6gonen,f%C3%B6r%20%C3%A5terbrukad%20kontorsinredning)  
* . Or show an office interior furnished with reused pieces looking modern (the Wihlborgs article likely has images of their reused-furnished spaces – use those).  
* For building materials, maybe show the Återbrukshub pilot outcomes (if any pictures public of reused building components in new building).  
* Any concept visuals we produce for them should highlight reused items not as second-rate but as focal points (like a cool vintage armchair in a modern room – thus flipping the narrative to positive uniqueness).  
* If possible, invite them to physically visit a reference: e.g., take them to Wihlborgs' office or to YLLW’s showroom to touch and feel the refurbished furniture. Tangible experience can convert skeptics.

Final Client Communications:

* Provide a Reused Content Report at project handover (from Phase 5 above), which they can use in their own marketing: e.g., how many kg of material reused, CO₂ saved, each item’s story (some hotels put little tags or menu cards telling the story of upcycled elements – consider providing content for that).  
* Prepare talking points or a press release draft for the hotel about their reuse initiative. Many may not have the know-how to articulate it; if we give them a polished narrative, they are more likely to promote it, which reinforces their commitment.

Addressing Internal Stakeholders:

* Sometimes not only clients but also within Fyra or among project consultants there could be hesitation. Educating our own project managers and the cost consultants about how we’ve covered risks and saved money ensures the whole team aligns when communicating to the client (no mixed messages like QS erroneously saying “used may cost more maintenance” which could spook client).  
* So the toolkit is also an internal one: training our team on reuse value proposition and giving them confidence to advocate it.

By using this communication approach, we aim to turn reuse from a perceived risk into a selling point. The key is to inform, reassure with evidence, and excite with possibilities. Once a client buys in and sees the results, they often become repeat adopters (e.g., Wihlborgs expanding reuse in multiple offices after success

[wihlborgs.se](https://www.wihlborgs.se/sv/om-oss/nyheter/hallbar-fastighetsutveckling/minskad-klimatpaverkan-med-aterbruk-av-kontorsmobler/#:~:text=Att%20samverka%20med%20hyresg%C3%A4ster%20kring,Wihlborgs%20s%C3%A5%20kallade%20Gr%C3%B6na%20hyresavtal)

).

### **Conclusion of Operational Playbook**

Armed with the above strategies – a collaborative multi-vendor approach, a phased sourcing plan, risk mitigations at each step, diligent cost control, and strong client communication – Fyra can systematically integrate reuse into hospitality projects. This not only helps meet sustainability goals and client budget targets, but also positions Fyra as an innovative leader in circular interior design, which is a growing market differentiator.
